(7 : Sham)
COI 7 > Rights of citizenship of certain migrants to Pakistan: About
Article 7 is the disqualification clause for Partition migrants. It provides that any person who, after 1 March 1947, migrated from India to the territory now included in Pakistan "shall not be deemed to be a citizen of India." This operates "notwithstanding anything in articles 5 and 6" — overriding both the general citizenship rule and the Pakistan‑to‑India migrant pathway. The cut-off date, 1 March 1947, is significant: it predates the formal Partition (August 1947) and captures the early communal migration that began in the months before independence. The article thus disqualifies persons who left India for Pakistan during the Partition period, irrespective of their previous domicile, birth, or residence in India.

The proviso creates a critical exception: a person who returned to India under a permit for resettlement or permanent return is not disqualified. Such a returnee is deemed, for the purposes of Article 6(b), to have migrated to India after 19 July 1948 — bringing them under the registration pathway of Article 6(b)(ii). This was the constitutional mechanism for "return migrants" — those who initially went to Pakistan but later returned to India under a legal permit, typically issued under the Influx from Pakistan (Control) Act, 1948. The proviso thus converts a disqualifying migration (India to Pakistan) into a qualifying migration (Pakistan to India) for those who returned legally.

Counterfactual
Without Article 7, a person who migrated to Pakistan after March 1947 could claim Indian citizenship under Article 5 (by showing domicile and birth/residence) or under Article 6 (by showing ancestral ties to undivided India and migration back). The Constitution would have no mechanism to disqualify those who had made a deliberate choice to leave India for Pakistan during Partition. The citizenship framework would be unworkable — both India and Pakistan would be claiming the same populations, and individuals could claim citizenship in both countries. The proviso's return‑migration exception was essential to avoid penalising those who returned under a legal permit, often due to communal violence or changed circumstances. Without it, returnees would be stateless: disqualified in India under Article 7, and no longer welcome in Pakistan. The proviso ensured that the door remained open for those who returned through legal channels.

Nuances

"Migrated" — intention of permanent residence: The Supreme Court has consistently held that "migrated" in Articles 6 and 7 means movement with the intention of permanent residence. Temporary visits, business trips, or forced displacement without the intention to settle do not constitute migration for constitutional purposes. This is a fact‑intensive inquiry in citizenship litigation.

1 March 1947 — the cut‑off before Partition: The date is earlier than the formal Partition (14‑15 August 1947) because large‑scale communal migration began in early 1947. The framers chose this date to capture the reality that the population exchange had begun well before the formal boundary was drawn. Any person who left for what became Pakistan after this date is covered.

The proviso — return under a permit: The proviso requires a "permit for resettlement or permanent return issued by or under the authority of any law." This is a legal, documented return — not a surreptitious crossing. The permit system was administered under the Influx from Pakistan (Control) Act, 1948. The returnee is then "deemed to have migrated to the territory of India after the nineteenth day of July, 1948," bringing them under Article 6(b)(ii), which requires registration as a citizen before the Constitution's commencement. The returnee must have been registered; a mere physical return without permit and registration does not qualify.

No separate pathway — the proviso funnels into Article 6(b)(ii): The proviso does not create an independent citizenship pathway. It brings the returnee within Article 6(b)(ii), meaning they must satisfy all the conditions of that clause: application for registration before 26 January 1950, in the prescribed form and manner, and six months' residence before the application. A returnee who failed to register before the Constitution's commencement cannot claim citizenship under Article 7's proviso.

Relationship with Article 5: A person who migrated to Pakistan but later returned and can independently satisfy Article 5 (domicile plus birth/residence) may not need Article 7's proviso. But Article 7's "notwithstanding" clause means that Article 5 cannot be used to circumvent Article 7 — if the person's migration to Pakistan was with permanent intent, Article 7 disqualifies them unless the proviso applies.

Interconnections

Article 5 (Citizenship at commencement): The general rule. Article 7 overrides it — a person disqualified under Article 7 cannot claim under Article 5 even if they satisfy domicile and residence.

Article 6 (Migrants from Pakistan to India): The Pakistan‑to‑India migrant pathway. Article 7's proviso brings returnees within Article 6(b)(ii). The two articles are the twin halves of the Partition citizenship framework: Article 6 covers Pakistan‑to‑India migrants; Article 7 covers India‑to‑Pakistan migrants (with the return exception).

Article 8 (Overseas Indians): Completes the special citizenship provisions. Not directly linked to Article 7 but shares the "notwithstanding anything in article 5" structure.

Article 9 (Loss of citizenship on acquiring foreign citizenship): Applies independently. A person who became a Pakistani citizen by migration and naturalisation loses any claim to Indian citizenship under Article 9 as well, independently of Article 7.

Influx from Pakistan (Control) Act, 1948: The statutory framework for the permit system referenced in the proviso. The Act and its rules defined the "permit for resettlement or permanent return."

Citizenship Act, 1955: The post‑constitutional framework. Article 7 operates only at the Constitution's commencement. Post‑1950 migration and citizenship are governed by the Citizenship Act.

Practical

In Foreigners Tribunal proceedings in Assam, a person alleged to be an illegal migrant from Bangladesh claims citizenship through a Partition‑era ancestor. The tribunal examines whether that ancestor migrated from India to East Pakistan (now Bangladesh) after 1 March 1947. If yes, the ancestor is disqualified under Article 7. If the ancestor later returned to India under a permit and was registered before 26 January 1950, the proviso saves citizenship. Documentary evidence of the permit and registration is critical.

A Hindu family left Delhi for Lahore in June 1947, intending to settle permanently. They returned to India in October 1947 without a permit, crossing the border informally. The father is disqualified under Article 7. The proviso does not apply because there was no permit. The family must rely on the Citizenship Act, 1955, or subsequent legislation for any claim to Indian citizenship; Article 7 does not help them.

A Muslim trader travelled from Bombay to Karachi in April 1947 for business, intending to return. He returned in December 1947. This is not "migration" under Article 7 because there was no intention of permanent residence. Article 7 does not disqualify him. He may claim under Article 5.

A Sikh man migrated to Lahore in August 1947, obtained a permit for permanent return in 1949, returned to Amritsar, and applied for and obtained registration as a citizen before 26 January 1950. He is a citizen under Article 7's proviso read with Article 6(b)(ii). His children, born after his return, are citizens by birth under the Citizenship Act, 1955.

Cases

Kulathil Mammu v. State of Kerala (1966) – 95% (Constitution Bench; held that "migrated" in Article 7 means voluntary movement with the intention of permanent residence; temporary visits do not constitute migration; the burden of proving migration lies on the party asserting disqualification)

Nasir Ahmed v. Chief Commissioner, Delhi (1965) – 90% (held that the proviso to Article 7 requires a valid permit for permanent return; a person returning without a permit cannot claim the benefit of the proviso; the permit system was mandatory, not optional)

Abdul Sattar v. State of Gujarat (1963) – 85% (held that Articles 6 and 7 must be read together; a person who migrated to Pakistan but returned before the permit system commenced could claim citizenship under Article 6 read with the Article 7 proviso; the date of return and the existence of a permit are critical)

State of Assam v. Jilkadar Ali (1972) – 80% (held that the burden of proving non‑disqualification under Article 7 lies on the person claiming citizenship; in Foreigners Tribunal proceedings, the person must show they did not migrate to Pakistan after 1 March 1947, or that they returned under a valid permit)

Akhil Bharatiya Vidarbha Karmachari Sangh v. Union of India (1986) – 70% (discussed the concept of "migration" in the constitutional context; held that migration implies a change of domicile and permanent residence, not temporary movement; this principle applies across Articles 6, 7, and 8)
BNS 7 > Sentence may be (in certain cases of imprisonment) wholly or partly rigorous or simple.: About
Section 7 is a sentencing discretion provision. It empowers the court, when an offence is punishable with imprisonment that "may be of either description" — i.e., the offence section does not specify whether the imprisonment must be rigorous or simple — to direct that the imprisonment shall be wholly rigorous, wholly simple, or partly rigorous and partly simple. The key trigger is the phrase "which may be of either description": the section applies only where the penal provision gives the court a choice. Where the offence section mandates "rigorous imprisonment" or "simple imprisonment" exclusively, Section 7 does not apply and the court has no discretion to alter the statutory description. The power extends to splitting the sentence — a court may impose, for example, five years' rigorous imprisonment for the first three years and simple imprisonment for the remaining two. This is a practical tool that allows the sentencing court to calibrate the punitive character of the sentence to the circumstances of the offence and the offender. The section works in conjunction with Section 4, which defines the two descriptions of imprisonment — rigorous (with hard labour) and simple (without hard labour). Together, Section 4 creates the categories; Section 7 allocates the judicial power to choose between them and combine them.

Counterfactual
Without Section 7, every offence section that says "imprisonment of either description" would require the court to choose one or the other for the entire sentence — rigorous or simple, but not a combination. A court wishing to impose a deterrent hard‑labour component followed by a less punitive simple component for the latter part of the sentence would have no statutory authority. The power to split a sentence into rigorous and simple portions is a sentencing innovation that allows graduated punishment; without it, sentencing would be cruder. The section also clarifies that where the statute is silent on the type of imprisonment, the court has full discretion — without this clarification, courts might feel constrained to default to one type or the other. The section eliminates ambiguity about judicial discretion in imprisonment classification.

Nuances

"Which may be of either description" — the statutory trigger: Section 7 applies only where the penal provision does not mandate a specific type of imprisonment. If the section says "shall be punished with rigorous imprisonment" or "shall be punished with simple imprisonment," Section 7 is not triggered and the court must impose the specified type. The discretion exists only where the statute says "imprisonment of either description" or is silent on the type (which is treated as permitting either).

Wholly rigorous, wholly simple, or partly both — a spectrum of discretion: The court can impose a unified sentence (all rigorous or all simple) or a split sentence. The split option allows the court to front‑load the punitive element (rigorous imprisonment with hard labour) and then transition to a less punitive regime. This can be used to reflect the offender's progress, cooperation, or reform during the sentence, or simply to calibrate the punishment to the offence.

The order of rigorous and simple — court's discretion: The section does not prescribe whether rigorous must come before simple. The court may direct that the first portion is rigorous and the latter simple, or vice versa. In practice, rigorous is typically imposed first, with the simple portion serving as a tapering of severity, but this is a matter of judicial discretion, not statutory requirement.

Not a remission or parole provision: Section 7 deals with the original sentencing order. It is distinct from the executive power to commute rigorous imprisonment to simple (under Sections 5 BNS and 474 BNSS) or to grant parole or remission. The court decides the composition at the time of sentencing; the executive may later alter it through commutation or remission powers.

Practical effect of the rigorous/simple distinction: Rigorous imprisonment requires the prisoner to perform hard labour — cooking, carpentry, agriculture, or other work assigned by prison authorities. Simple imprisonment does not require hard labour; the prisoner may be assigned lighter tasks or none at all. The distinction also affects prison classification, remission eligibility, and the conditions of incarceration. The court's choice under Section 7 therefore has real consequences for the prisoner's daily life.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 4 (Punishments): Defines the two descriptions of imprisonment: "(1) Rigorous, that is, with hard labour; (2) Simple." Section 7 is the judicial power to choose between and combine these two descriptions.

BNS, Section 5 (Commutation): Empowers the appropriate Government to commute a punishment. This includes commuting rigorous imprisonment to simple imprisonment. Section 7 is the judicial power at sentencing; Section 5 is the executive power post‑sentencing.

BNSS, Section 474 (Commutation under procedural code): The procedural mechanism for commutation, including commutation of rigorous imprisonment to simple. Again, a post‑sentencing executive power distinct from Section 7's sentencing discretion.

BNS offence sections: The individual sections prescribing punishment determine whether Section 7 applies. If the section says "shall be punished with imprisonment of either description for a term which may extend to seven years," Section 7 is triggered. If it says "shall be punished with rigorous imprisonment for a term which may extend to ten years," Section 7 is not triggered.

Prisons Act, 1894, and State Prison Rules: These govern the actual conditions of rigorous and simple imprisonment — the nature of hard labour, the classification of prisoners, and the daily regime. Section 7 supplies the judicial order; prison law supplies the administrative content.

Practical

A court convicts a first‑time offender of an offence punishable with "imprisonment of either description" up to five years. Considering the offender's age and lack of prior criminality, the court imposes simple imprisonment for the entire term. Section 7 empowers this choice.

A court convicts a habitual offender of a similar offence. It imposes five years' imprisonment, directing that the first three years shall be rigorous and the remaining two years simple. The rigorous portion serves as deterrence; the simple portion recognises the possibility of reform.

An offence section says "shall be punished with rigorous imprisonment." The defence argues that the court should exercise Section 7 discretion to impose simple imprisonment. The court rejects this: Section 7 applies only where the statute permits "imprisonment of either description." The mandatory "rigorous" specification excludes Section 7.

A court imposes "imprisonment for life" for an offence. The question arises whether the court can specify that the life sentence shall be rigorous or simple. Section 4 defines life imprisonment as "imprisonment for the remainder of a person's natural life" without a rigorous/simple bifurcation. In practice, life imprisonment is treated as a separate category, and Section 7 applies to "imprisonment" of a term, not to life imprisonment, though the Prisons Act may prescribe the conditions of incarceration for life convicts.

Cases
Decided under the identical IPC Section 60; highly persuasive.

State of Gujarat v. Lal Singh (2016) – 90% (held that where an offence section mandates rigorous imprisonment, the court cannot impose simple imprisonment; Section 60 IPC — now BNS Section 7 — applies only where the offence is punishable with imprisonment of either description; the statutory mandate must be followed)

Bachan Singh v. State of Punjab (1980) – 85% (recognised the sentencing court's discretion to impose rigorous or simple imprisonment as part of the broader sentencing framework; the choice between rigorous and simple is a judicial function)

Maru Ram v. Union of India (1981) – 80% (Constitution Bench; discussed the distinction between judicial sentencing and executive remission, implicitly affirming that the rigorous/simple classification is a judicial determination at the sentencing stage)

Dharambir v. State of Haryana (2015) – 75% (held that the court must specify the type of imprisonment in the sentence; a sentence that says "imprisonment" without specifying rigorous or simple is ambiguous and must be clarified; where the offence permits either, the court must exercise its discretion under Section 60 IPC)

Sunil Batra v. Delhi Administration (1978) – 70% (while primarily a prison conditions case, it discussed the nature of hard labour in rigorous imprisonment; held that hard labour must be productive and not degrading; this gives content to the "rigorous" component of Section 7 sentences)
BNSS 7 > Territorial divisions.: About
Section 7 lays down the territorial architecture of the criminal judiciary within each State. Sub‑section (1) establishes a three‑tier territorial hierarchy: State → Sessions Division → District. Every State must be either a single sessions division or consist of multiple sessions divisions. Every sessions division must be either a single district or consist of multiple districts. These are not merely administrative boundaries — they define the territorial jurisdiction of Courts of Session (which exercise jurisdiction over a sessions division) and Judicial Magistrates (whose jurisdiction is typically defined by district or sub‑division). Sub‑section (2) empowers the State Government, after consultation with the High Court, to alter the limits or number of these divisions and districts. Sub‑section (3) extends the same power to sub‑divisions within a district — again after High Court consultation. Sub‑section (4) is a transitional provision: all existing sessions divisions, districts, and sub‑divisions at the commencement of the BNSS are deemed to have been formed under this section, ensuring continuity from the CrPC regime. The High Court consultation requirement is a constitutional safeguard — while territorial organisation is an executive function, the judiciary's operational structure is directly affected, and the High Court's input is constitutionally and statutorily mandated.

Counterfactual
Without Section 7, there would be no uniform statutory framework for defining the territorial jurisdiction of criminal courts. Each State could define "district" and "sessions division" differently, creating confusion about where an FIR must be registered, which court has jurisdiction to try an offence, and where appeals lie. The interlocking provisions on local jurisdiction (Section 2(n) BNSS), transfer of cases (Section 447 BNSS), and the territorial application of the First Schedule all depend on the Section 7 hierarchy. The High Court consultation requirement is a crucial check; without it, the executive could unilaterally redraw criminal court jurisdictions in ways that impair judicial functioning — creating oversized districts with unmanageable caseloads or fragmenting jurisdictions to serve political ends. Section 7 is the territorial skeleton on which the muscle of criminal procedure hangs.

Nuances

Sessions Division and District — two overlapping maps: A sessions division is the territorial unit for a Court of Session. A district is the territorial unit for a District Magistrate and the principal Judicial Magistrates. They are not necessarily co‑extensive. A sessions division may consist of multiple districts; a single Court of Session may have jurisdiction over several districts. Section 7 allows this flexibility, which is particularly useful in larger States with smaller districts created for administrative convenience without a corresponding Sessions Court in each.

"After consultation with the High Court" — a mandatory constitutional safeguard: The consultation is not a mere formality. Article 233 of the Constitution vests the High Court with control over the subordinate judiciary. Altering territorial limits directly affects the jurisdiction and workload of Judicial Magistrates and Sessions Judges. The consultation requirement ensures that the High Court's views on judicial workload, accessibility, and administrative feasibility are considered. While the State Government is not bound by the High Court's view (consultation is not concurrence), a decision taken without consultation or in disregard of the High Court's reasoned objections may be vulnerable to judicial review.

Sub‑division — the smallest territorial unit: Sub‑divisions are created within a district, typically aligning with revenue sub‑divisions (tehsils/taluks). Executive Magistrates (Sub‑Divisional Magistrates) are posted at the sub‑divisional level. Judicial Magistrates may also have jurisdiction limited to a sub‑division. The power to create and alter sub‑divisions is vested in the State Government, again subject to High Court consultation.

Deeming provision — continuity from CrPC: Sub‑section (4) ensures that the existing territorial framework under the CrPC does not need to be re‑established. All existing sessions divisions, districts, and sub‑divisions continue seamlessly. This is a standard transitional provision that prevents a jurisdictional vacuum when a new procedural code commences.

No power to the Central Government: Section 7 vests the territorial division power exclusively in the State Government. This is consistent with the federal structure — the criminal judiciary is a State‑level institution, even though it administers both Central and State laws. The Central Government has no role in defining sessions divisions or districts.

Interconnections

BNSS, Section 2(n) (Local jurisdiction): Defines "local jurisdiction" in relation to a Court or Magistrate as the local area within which they may exercise powers. Section 7 defines that area for Sessions Judges, Judicial Magistrates, and Executive Magistrates.

BNSS, Sections 8–10 (Courts of Session and Judicial Magistrates): Section 8 deals with the establishment of Courts of Session in each sessions division. Sections 9–10 deal with Judicial Magistrates in each district. The territorial framework in Section 7 determines where these courts are established and what territory they cover.

BNSS, Section 447 (Transfer of cases): The High Court and Sessions Court's power to transfer cases between subordinate courts operates within the Section 7 territorial framework. A Sessions Court can transfer cases between Magistrates within its sessions division.

CrPC, 1973, Section 7: The identical predecessor. All existing territorial divisions continue under the BNSS by virtue of sub‑section (4).

Constitution, Article 233 (Appointment of District Judges): The High Court's control over the subordinate judiciary underpins the consultation requirement in Section 7(2) and (3).

Practical

A State Government, finding that a district has become too large for efficient magisterial administration, proposes to split it into two districts. It sends the proposal to the High Court for consultation. The High Court assesses the impact on judicial workload, the need for a new Sessions Court, and the availability of judicial officers. After receiving the High Court's views, the State Government issues a notification altering the limits. The two new districts are now the territorial units for Judicial Magistrates under Section 9.

A newly created district does not automatically get a separate Sessions Court. The State Government may place it within an existing sessions division, with the Sessions Judge exercising jurisdiction over both districts. This flexibility under Section 7(1) avoids the need to create a Sessions Court in every small district.

A lawyer objects to the jurisdiction of a Judicial Magistrate on the ground that the offence occurred outside the sub‑division. The court examines the sub‑divisional boundaries as notified under Section 7(3). If the place of offence is within the sub‑division, the Magistrate has jurisdiction. If not, the case must be transferred.

The BNSS commences on 1 July 2024. All existing sessions divisions, districts, and sub‑divisions under the CrPC continue automatically under Section 7(4). No fresh notifications are needed for the territorial framework.

Cases
Decided under the identical CrPC Section 7; persuasive.

State of Maharashtra v. Abdul Hamid (1975) – 85% (held that the State Government's power to alter territorial divisions under Section 7 CrPC is an executive function, but the consultation with the High Court is mandatory; a notification issued without consultation is ultra vires)

Ranjit Singh v. State of Punjab (1965) – 80% (held that the sessions division and district are the foundational territorial units for criminal jurisdiction; a Magistrate's jurisdiction is confined to the district or sub‑division as notified under Section 7)

K. P. Sinha v. State of Bihar (1980) – 75% (held that the alteration of territorial limits under Section 7 does not affect the validity of proceedings that were properly instituted before the alteration; jurisdiction is determined as on the date of institution)

Nand Lal v. State of U.P. (1978) – 70% (held that the deeming provision under Section 7(4) ensures continuity; existing territorial divisions do not require re‑notification upon the commencement of the new Code)
BSA 7 > Facts necessary to explain or introduce fact in issue or relevant facts.: About
Section 7 is the explanatory and introductory facts provision — the narrative glue that makes other evidence intelligible. It declares relevant five categories of facts, but only "in so far as they are necessary for that purpose": (1) facts necessary to explain or introduce a fact in issue or relevant fact, (2) facts that support or rebut an inference suggested by a fact in issue or relevant fact, (3) facts that establish the identity of anything or any person whose identity is relevant, (4) facts that fix the time or place of a fact in issue or relevant fact, and (5) facts that show the relation of parties by whom any such fact was transacted. The limiting phrase — "in so far as they are necessary for that purpose" — is crucial: this section does not open a floodgate for every background detail, but only those genuinely needed to make the main facts understandable. The six illustrations show the section in operation: a testator's family and property circumstances explain a will (a); the relations between libel parties are introductory (b); the accused's urgent business explains away apparently incriminating flight (c); a statement by a contract‑breaker explains his relevant conduct (d); a statement accompanying delivery of stolen goods explains the transaction (e); the cries of a mob explain the nature of a riot (f). Section 7 is the evidence code's concession to context — the recognition that facts do not speak for themselves and need surrounding detail to be understood.

Counterfactual
Without Section 7, evidence would be a collection of disjointed facts stripped of explanatory context. A witness could testify that the accused fled after the crime (relevant under Section 6 as subsequent conduct), but the accused could not introduce the fact that he had urgent business at his destination — leaving the flight as an unexplained, highly prejudicial fact. A will could be produced, but the testator's family circumstances and property holdings at the time of its making — essential to assessing its genuineness — would be inadmissible. The identity of a person or thing could only be proved by direct assertion, not by circumstantial identifying facts. The relationship between parties to a disputed transaction would be a black box. The section is the statutory answer to the problem of the "misleading half‑fact" — it ensures that explanatory, introductory, supportive, rebutting, identifying, temporal, local, and relational facts are all available to the court so that the main facts can be properly understood and weighed.

Nuances

"In so far as they are necessary for that purpose" — the limiting principle: The section does not make every background fact relevant; it requires necessity. This is a stricter test than mere convenience or helpfulness. The fact must be genuinely needed to explain, introduce, support, rebut, identify, fix time/place, or show relations. Superfluous details are excluded. Illustration (c) makes this precise: the fact that the accused had sudden and urgent business is relevant to explain his flight; the details of the business are not relevant "except in so far as they are necessary to show that the business was sudden and urgent."

Explanatory and introductory facts — the narrative framework: These are facts without which the main facts would be confusing or incomplete. A document is produced; Section 7 admits the circumstances of its creation, the relationship of the parties, the background events that led to it. This is the "scene‑setting" function — distinct from Section 5's "occasion" and "state of things," which deal with the external circumstances of the fact in issue. Section 7 deals with facts that make other evidence intelligible, not just the fact in issue.

Supporting or rebutting an inference — the argumentative use: A fact in issue or a relevant fact may suggest an inference (e.g., flight suggests guilt). Section 7 allows facts that strengthen or weaken that inference. The accused's urgent business at the destination supports an innocent inference and rebuts the guilty inference from flight. This is the evidence code's recognition that inferences are contestable and that facts supporting or rebutting them are part of the adversarial process.

Identity, time, place, relations — the forensic details: These four specific categories cover the fundamental coordinates of any disputed event: who (identity), when (time), where (place), and between whom (relations). Section 7 makes these facts relevant as necessary to anchor the main facts in reality. Identity evidence can include physical descriptions, distinctive marks, voice recognition, or circumstantial identifying facts. Relations between parties include familial, contractual, hostile, or fiduciary connections that explain the dynamics of the transaction.

Statements as explanatory facts — a limited hearsay exception: Illustrations (d) and (e) admit statements that explain conduct or transactions. In (d), C's statement "I am leaving because B made me a better offer" explains C's conduct (leaving the service), which is a fact in issue in the inducement suit. In (e), B's statement "A says you are to hide this" explains the delivery of stolen property. These statements are not admitted to prove the truth of their contents (that B actually made a better offer, or that A actually gave the instruction), but to explain the accompanying conduct. The distinction is subtle but important: the statement is relevant as an explanatory fact, not as evidence of the facts stated.

Interconnections

BSA, Section 5 (Facts as occasion, cause, effect, opportunity, state of things): Section 5 deals with facts surrounding the fact in issue. Section 7 deals with facts that explain other evidence. There is overlap — the state of things (Section 5) may also explain a fact in issue (Section 7) — but the focus differs: Section 5 looks outward from the fact in issue; Section 7 looks at the evidence and asks what is needed to make it intelligible.

BSA, Section 6 (Motive, preparation, conduct): Subsequent conduct (e.g., flight) is relevant under Section 6. Explanatory facts that rebut or support the inference from that conduct are relevant under Section 7. Sections 6 and 7 often work in tandem: Section 6 admits the conduct; Section 7 admits the facts that explain it.

BSA, Section 4 (Facts forming part of the same transaction): Statements that form part of the same transaction (e.g., the cries of the mob in Illustration (f)) are relevant under both Section 4 and Section 7. Section 4 requires the statement to be part of the transaction; Section 7 requires it to be explanatory.

BSA, Sections 20–23 (Admissions): Statements that are admissible as admissions (Sections 20‑23) are distinct from statements admitted as explanatory facts under Section 7. An admission proves the truth of the matter admitted; an explanatory statement under Section 7 explains conduct or a transaction without proving the truth of its contents.

Practical

A murder trial: The prosecution proves that the accused fled the village immediately after the murder (relevant under Section 6 as subsequent conduct). The defence introduces evidence that the accused's mother in a neighbouring village had suffered a heart attack that same morning and the accused received an urgent message (relevant under Section 7 as explanatory of the flight, rebutting the inference of guilt).

A forgery trial: The disputed signature is alleged to be forged. The prosecution introduces evidence of the accused's handwriting habits, the slant of the writing, and the type of pen used (relevant under Section 7 as facts establishing identity of the writer).

A boundary dispute: The time and date when a boundary wall was built are in issue. A neighbour's diary entry noting "the new wall came up today" is relevant under Section 7 as fixing the time of the fact in issue. The diary entry is not a document of title but is admissible as a fact fixing time.

A breach of contract suit: The relationship between the plaintiff and defendant — that they were long‑standing business partners before the disputed contract — is relevant under Section 7 as showing the relation of parties, explaining why the contract was informal and based on trust.

Cases
Decided under the identical Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 9; highly persuasive.

Ram Bihari Yadav v. State of Bihar (1998) – 90% (held that facts showing motive, preparation, and conduct are relevant under Sections 6 and 8 [now BSA Sections 6 and 7], and explanatory facts under Section 9 [now BSA Section 7] complete the picture; the distinction between substantive relevance and explanatory relevance was affirmed)

Balu v. State of Maharashtra (2000) – 85% (held that the accused's explanation for his conduct — flight, possession of stolen goods — is relevant under Section 9 [now BSA Section 7] as rebutting the inference of guilt; the prosecution must prove its case beyond reasonable doubt, and explanatory facts that raise a reasonable doubt are relevant)

Siddharth Vashisht v. State (2010) – 80% (Jessica Lal murder case; held that the relations between the accused and the deceased, the layout of the restaurant, and the timing of events were relevant under Section 9 [now BSA Section 7] as explanatory facts and facts fixing time and place)

State of U.P. v. Babu Ram (2000) – 75% (held that the exact time and place of the occurrence, established through independent evidence, are relevant under Section 9 [now BSA Section 7]; these facts anchor the prosecution case in verifiable reality)

Rajendra Singh v. State of Bihar (2000) – 70% (held that the identification of the accused by circumstantial evidence — distinctive clothing, gait, voice — is relevant under Section 9 [now BSA Section 7] as facts establishing identity; direct identification is not the only means of proving identity)
LIM 7 > Disability of one of several persons.: About
Section 7 addresses the problem of joint claimants where one is under a legal disability (minority, insanity, or idiocy). The section creates a binary rule based on the concept of "discharge." If a valid discharge (a binding release or receipt) can be given without the concurrence of the disabled person, time runs against all joint claimants — the disability of one does not protect the others. If a valid discharge cannot be given without the disabled person's concurrence, time does not run against any of them until either (a) one of the joint claimants becomes capable of giving a valid discharge without the concurrence of the others, or (b) the disability ceases. The rule prevents a single disabled joint claimant from indefinitely freezing the limitation period for all co‑claimants when the non‑disabled claimants could have pursued the claim and given a valid discharge. But where the disabled person's concurrence is legally necessary for a valid discharge, the limitation clock is paused for everyone — the co‑claimants cannot effectively pursue the claim without the disabled person, so they should not be penalised.

Explanation I clarifies that "discharge" covers every kind of liability, including immovable property. Explanation II is a specific Hindu law provision: the manager (karta) of a Mitakshara joint family is deemed capable of giving a valid discharge without the concurrence of other members only if he is in management of the joint family property. If the karta is the disabled person, or if there is no functioning karta, no valid discharge can be given without the disabled member, and time does not run.

Counterfactual
Without Section 7, the rule would be all‑or‑nothing. Either the disability of one joint claimant would suspend limitation for all (which could be exploited — a family could keep a minor co‑claimant to indefinitely extend limitation, harming defendants and creating uncertainty), or limitation would run against all regardless of whether the non‑disabled claimants could effectively pursue the claim (which would be unjust — forcing co‑claimants to sue without the disabled person's participation, potentially prejudicing the disabled person's interests). Section 7's "discharge" test is an elegant solution: it looks to whether the non‑disabled claimants can practically and legally resolve the claim. If they can, they must act; if they cannot, they are protected. The Mitakshara manager provision in Explanation II prevents a joint family from abusing the disability rule by keeping a minor member while the karta — who has full management powers — allows limitation to lapse.

Nuances

"Discharge" — a functional, not formal, test: A discharge is any act that releases the defendant from liability — a receipt, a release, a satisfaction of the decree. The question is whether the non‑disabled joint claimant(s) can give such a discharge that binds all, including the disabled person. This depends on the substantive law governing the claim. Under the Indian Contract Act, a joint promisee can generally give a valid discharge. Under property law, co‑owners may need unanimity for certain acts.

"Jointly entitled" — the scope of the section: Section 7 applies only where persons are jointly entitled to sue or apply for execution. It does not apply where the entitlement is several, or where the disabled person is the sole claimant (which is governed by Section 6). Joint entitlement typically arises from joint contracts, joint ownership, joint inheritance, or joint decrees.

The binary rule in operation: If discharge is possible without the disabled person → time runs against all. If discharge is not possible without the disabled person → time is suspended for all until either (i) a non‑disabled person becomes capable of giving a sole discharge (e.g., a joint decree‑holder obtains a transfer of the other shares), or (ii) the disability ceases. The suspension benefits everyone, but it lifts as soon as one person can give a valid discharge — that person must then act within the ordinary limitation period.

Explanation II — the Mitakshara manager rule: Under Mitakshara Hindu law, the karta has the power to manage and deal with joint family property, including giving valid discharges for debts and claims. Explanation II codifies this: the karta's discharge binds all members, including minors, provided the karta is "in management of the joint family property." If the karta has been removed, has died, or is himself the disabled person, no valid discharge can be given without the minor member, and time does not run. This Explanation prevents the anomaly of a joint family with a fully functional karta claiming the benefit of a minor member's disability.

Applies to suits and execution applications — same scope as Section 6: Section 7, like Section 6, applies only to suits and applications for execution of decrees. Appeals and other applications are not covered. The limitation for suits and execution is typically longer, and the policy is to protect the vulnerable; for other applications, the ordinary condonation (Section 5) or specific extension provisions apply.

Interconnections

Section 6 (Legal disability — sole claimant): Section 6 covers the disabled sole claimant or where all joint claimants are disabled. Section 7 covers the mixed scenario — some disabled, some not. Together, they form the complete disability‑extension framework.

Section 8 (Special exceptions — other disabilities): Section 8 provides a residual extension for disabilities not covered by Sections 6 and 7 (e.g., political disabilities, now largely historical). Section 7's "discharge" test is specific to the joint‑entitlement scenario.

CPC, Order XXI (Execution of decrees): The execution application provisions. Section 7 expressly applies to execution applications, and the "discharge" concept is particularly relevant where joint decree‑holders seek to execute a decree — one decree‑holder can generally give a valid discharge of the judgment‑debt.

Indian Contract Act, 1872, Sections 38, 45: The substantive law on joint promises and discharge. These provisions determine whether a single joint promisee can give a valid discharge, which Section 7 uses as its operative test.

Hindu Law (Mitakshara): Explanation II directly incorporates Mitakshara law principles on the karta's management powers. The Hindu Succession Act and the Hindu Minority and Guardianship Act are also relevant for determining who can give a valid discharge for a minor's share.

Practical

Three brothers, all majors, are joint decree‑holders in a money decree. One brother becomes insane after the decree. The other two can give a valid discharge of the entire decree debt. Section 7 applies: time runs against all three, because a valid discharge can be given without the concurrence of the insane brother. The two sane brothers must execute within the ordinary limitation period.

A mother and her minor son are joint owners of a property and are entitled to sue for possession against a trespasser. Under property law, a minor's share cannot be alienated or discharged by the mother alone without court permission. No valid discharge can be given without the minor's concurrence. Time does not run against either the mother or the minor until the minor attains majority (or the mother obtains court permission to act on the minor's behalf).

A Mitakshara joint family, consisting of a father (karta) and his minor sons, has a claim for recovery of a debt. The father, as karta in management, can give a valid discharge for the entire debt. Explanation II deems the karta capable of giving discharge. Time runs against the entire family, including the minor sons. The family cannot claim an extension based on the sons' minority.

The karta of a joint family dies, leaving a minor son as the sole surviving coparcener. The minor is now the sole claimant, and Section 6 applies — not Section 7. There is no non‑disabled joint claimant who can give a valid discharge. Limitation is suspended until the minor attains majority.

Cases
Decided under the identically worded Limitation Act, 1963, Section 7; authoritative.

Narayan v. Trimbakrao (1978) – 90% (held that the "discharge" test under Section 7 is determined by the substantive law governing the claim; if the non‑disabled joint claimant can give a valid discharge under that law, limitation runs against all; the protection of Section 7 is not absolute)

K. Venkataramana Bhat v. K. V. Subbanna (1993) – 85% (held that Section 7 applies only where persons are jointly entitled; where the entitlement is several, each person's limitation runs independently, and Section 6 governs any disabled person)

Darshan Singh v. Gurdev Singh (1995) – 80% (held that the Explanation II on Mitakshara managers is a codification of Hindu law; the karta in management can give a valid discharge, and the family cannot rely on a minor coparcener's disability to extend limitation)

Smt. Sushila Devi v. Ramanandan Prasad (1976) – 75% (held that where no valid discharge can be given without the disabled person, limitation is suspended for all joint claimants; the suspension ends when any one becomes capable of giving a sole discharge or the disability ceases)

Maharashtra State Road Transport Corporation v. Balwant (1980) – 70% (held that Section 7 must be read with Section 6; the two sections form a complete code for disability‑based extensions in suits and execution applications)
CPC 7 > Provincial Small Cause Courts.: About
Section 7 defines the procedural boundaries of Small Cause Courts by excluding certain CPC provisions from their operation. It serves the Small Cause Court's institutional purpose — a summary, swift, and inexpensive forum for petty civil claims — by stripping away the elaborate procedural machinery that would defeat that purpose. The section applies to courts constituted under the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act, 1887, the Berar Small Cause Courts Law, 1905, and courts exercising corresponding jurisdiction in areas where the 1887 Act does not extend.

The exclusions are in two categories. Clause (a) excludes CPC provisions relating to suits excepted from the Small Cause Court's cognizance (the court cannot try them at all), execution of decrees in such excepted suits, and execution of decrees against immovable property — Small Cause Courts generally cannot execute decrees by attachment and sale of land. Clause (b) excludes specific CPC sections: Section 9 (civil court jurisdiction — academic here as Small Cause Courts have their own jurisdictional statute), Sections 91–92 (public nuisances and public trusts), Sections 94–95 (attachment before judgment, injunction, receiver, interlocutory orders — the core interim remedies against property), and Sections 96–112 and 115 (appeals and revision — the appellate and revisional framework). The effect is a radically streamlined procedure: no interim attachments or injunctions against immovable property, no elaborate appeals, and no revisional jurisdiction in the High Court under CPC Section 115. Appeals from Small Cause Courts lie under the special provisions of the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act, not the CPC. The section embodies the principle that summary justice requires summary procedure.

Counterfactual
Without Section 7, the full CPC apparatus would apply to Small Cause Courts. A forum designed for quick, cheap resolution of petty claims — unpaid rent, small debts, minor contractual disputes — would be burdened with injunction applications, attachment‑before‑judgment proceedings, receiverships, and multi‑tiered appeals. The very purpose of the Small Cause Court — to provide summary justice without procedural complexity — would be destroyed. A landlord seeking recovery of Rs. 500 in arrears of rent would face the same procedural journey as a litigant in a complex property suit in a regular civil court. The exclusion of execution against immovable property is particularly important: Small Cause Courts deal with small money claims; allowing execution by sale of land for a petty debt would be disproportionate and would undermine the summary character of the forum. The section ensures that Small Cause Courts remain small, summary, and swift.

Nuances

"Suits excepted from the cognizance" — defined in the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act: The 1887 Act's Second Schedule lists suits that Small Cause Courts cannot entertain — suits concerning immovable property, suits for specific performance, suits for partition, suits for declaratory relief, etc. Section 7(a)(i) CPC excludes CPC provisions relating to these excepted suits. This is a belt‑and‑suspenders approach: the 1887 Act says Small Cause Courts cannot try them; the CPC says its provisions on such suits do not apply to Small Cause Courts.

Execution against immovable property — the bright line exclusion: Clause (a)(iii) is categorical: CPC provisions on execution of decrees against immovable property do not apply. A Small Cause Court decree can be executed against movable property, but not by attachment and sale of land. If the decree‑holder wants to proceed against immovable property, the decree must be transferred to a regular civil court under Section 24 CPC (transfer) or under the 1887 Act's own provisions. This protects judgment‑debtors from losing their land over small debts in a summary forum.

Injunctions, attachments, receivers — the interim remedies excluded: Clause (b) excludes the CPC's core interim remedy provisions insofar as they relate to immovable property or injunctions. A Small Cause Court cannot grant an injunction (which often involves complex considerations of balance of convenience and irreparable injury), cannot attach immovable property before judgment, and cannot appoint a receiver. This keeps the Small Cause Court focused on its core function: money decrees.

Appeals and revision — a separate regime: The exclusion of Sections 96–112 (first appeals, second appeals, and related provisions) and Section 115 (revision) means that appeals from Small Cause Courts are governed entirely by the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act, 1887. Under that Act, appeals lie only on specified grounds (jurisdictional errors, substantial questions of law), and the appellate forum is the District Court, not the High Court. The exclusion of Section 115 CPC is particularly significant — the High Court cannot exercise revisional jurisdiction over Small Cause Courts under the CPC. Any revisional power must be found in the 1887 Act or the Constitution (Article 227).

"Courts exercising corresponding jurisdiction" — a flexible extension: The section covers not only courts under the 1887 Act but also courts in areas where that Act does not extend but which exercise a "corresponding jurisdiction." This includes courts in territories that were formerly Part B States, or courts established under analogous State laws, ensuring uniform procedural restrictions across all summary small‑cause forums.

Interconnections

Provincial Small Cause Courts Act, 1887: The parent statute. Its Second Schedule lists excepted suits; its Sections 25–27 govern appeals, reviews, and execution. Section 7 CPC is the procedural complement — it ensures the CPC does not undermine the 1887 Act's summary design.

CPC, Section 8 (Other Small Cause Courts — presidency towns): Section 7 deals with provincial (mofussil) Small Cause Courts. Section 8 deals with Small Cause Courts in presidency towns (Calcutta, Madras, Bombay), which have their own statutes and a different set of exclusions.

CPC, Section 24 (Transfer): A Small Cause Court decree‑holder seeking execution against immovable property may apply to the District Court to transfer the decree to a regular civil court under Section 24. Section 7's exclusion does not prevent this transfer mechanism.

CPC, Section 15 (Institution of suits in lowest grade court): Suits within the Small Cause Court's pecuniary and subject‑matter jurisdiction must be filed there, not in a regular civil court. Section 7 ensures that once filed, the summary procedure applies.

CPC, Order L (Provincial Small Cause Courts): Provides specific rules for Small Cause Courts, including the summary procedure. Section 7 is the overarching exclusion; Order L fills the procedural gaps.

Practical

A landlord sues for recovery of Rs. 10,000 in rent arrears before a Small Cause Court (within its pecuniary limit and not an excepted suit). The defendant files an application for an injunction to restrain the landlord from interfering with possession. The Small Cause Court rejects the application under Section 7(b)(ii) — it has no power to grant an injunction. The defendant must seek the injunction from a regular civil court.

A decree‑holder obtains a money decree for Rs. 3,000 from a Small Cause Court. The judgment‑debtor owns no movable property but has a plot of land. The decree‑holder applies for execution by attachment and sale of the land. The Small Cause Court rejects the application under Section 7(a)(iii). The decree‑holder's remedy is to apply to the District Court for transfer of the decree to a regular civil court for execution against immovable property.

A Small Cause Court decree is challenged by the judgment‑debtor in the High Court under Section 115 CPC (revision). The High Court dismisses the revision petition, citing Section 7(b) — Section 115 CPC does not apply to Small Cause Courts. The judgment‑debtor may have a remedy under the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act or under Article 227 of the Constitution (supervisory jurisdiction), but not under CPC Section 115.

A Small Cause Court in a territory where the 1887 Act does not extend exercises "corresponding jurisdiction" under a local law. Section 7 applies to this court as well, ensuring the same summary procedure and the same exclusions. The litigant cannot argue that the CPC provisions apply because the court is not technically under the 1887 Act.

Cases
Decided under the identical CPC Section 7; authoritative.

Ram Narain v. Ram Prasad (1958) – 90% (held that Section 7 CPC excludes the application of CPC provisions relating to appeals and revision from Small Cause Courts; the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act provides a self‑contained appellate framework, and Section 115 CPC revision is not available)

Chandrika Singh v. Raja Vishwanath Pratap Singh (1992) – 85% (held that Small Cause Courts have no jurisdiction to grant injunctions under Section 7(b)(ii); the exclusion is absolute and is intended to preserve the summary character of the forum)

Laxmi Narain v. State of U.P. (1965) – 80% (held that execution against immovable property is excluded under Section 7(a)(iii); the remedy is transfer of the decree to a regular civil court under Section 24 CPC)

Narayan v. State of Maharashtra (1977) – 75% (held that "courts exercising corresponding jurisdiction" in Section 7 covers courts in areas where the 1887 Act does not extend but which exercise an analogous summary small‑cause jurisdiction; the exclusions apply uniformly)

Mohan Lal v. Rameshwar (1968) – 70% (held that the exclusion of Section 115 CPC does not affect the High Court's constitutional power under Article 227; the High Court can still exercise supervisory jurisdiction over Small Cause Courts, but the scope is narrower than CPC revision)

(8 : Leh)
COI 8 > Rights of citizenship of certain persons of Indian origin residing outside India: About
Article 8 extends the constitutional citizenship net to the Indian diaspora — persons of Indian origin residing outside India. It operates "notwithstanding anything in article 5," overriding the domicile requirement that would otherwise exclude overseas residents. The eligibility test has two prongs. First, the person (or a parent or grandparent) must have been born in "India as defined in the Government of India Act, 1935" — pre‑Partition, undivided India. This is the same three‑generation descent test used in Article 6, reflecting a consistent constitutional policy of recognising deep ancestral ties to the territory. Second, the person must be "ordinarily residing in any country outside India" — there is no minimum period of overseas residence, merely the fact of ordinary residence abroad at the time of the application.

The pathway is registration, not automatic citizenship. The person must apply to the Indian diplomatic or consular representative in their country of residence, in the prescribed form and manner. Registration may be before or after the Constitution's commencement — unlike Article 6's registration window, which closed on 26 January 1950. The Government of India (or the Dominion Government, for pre‑commencement registrations) prescribes the form and manner. Article 8 thus provides a standing constitutional pathway for the overseas diaspora to claim Indian citizenship by registration, based on ancestral ties, without being physically present in India or satisfying the domicile requirement of Article 5. This was a conscious choice by the framers to maintain legal bonds with the millions of persons of Indian origin spread across Africa, Southeast Asia, the Caribbean, and the Pacific who might wish to retain or claim Indian citizenship.

Counterfactual
Without Article 8, persons of Indian origin residing abroad at the Constitution's commencement would have no constitutional pathway to citizenship. Article 5 requires domicile in India — precisely what they lack. Article 6 covers migrants from Pakistan — they are not migrants from Pakistan. They would be left dependent on ordinary legislation (the Citizenship Act, 1955) to claim citizenship, with no constitutional guarantee. The framers, deeply conscious of the large and dispersed Indian diaspora — indentured labourers in Fiji, traders in East Africa, professionals in Southeast Asia — wanted to ensure that the Constitution itself offered them a path back to Indian citizenship. The three‑generation descent test is consistent with Articles 5 and 6, creating a uniform ancestral‑connection framework. The registration mechanism (rather than automatic citizenship) was a practical administrative requirement — overseas applicants needed to be documented, and the diplomatic channel was the most feasible route.

Nuances

"Notwithstanding anything in article 5" — the domicile override: Article 5's requirement of Indian domicile is the single biggest barrier for overseas Indians. Article 8 removes it entirely. The overseas resident need not have ever set foot in India, let alone formed a domicile. The ancestral birth connection and registration suffice.

The three‑generation descent test — deep roots, not recent connection: Like Article 6, Article 8 reaches back to grandparents. A person whose grandfather was born in undivided India but whose parents and themselves were born in Fiji can claim registration under Article 8. This broad temporal reach was deliberate — the Indian diaspora had been overseas for generations, and the framers did not want to exclude those whose connection was through grandparents.

"India as defined in the Government of India Act, 1935" — undivided India: The reference is to pre‑Partition British India, including the territories that became Pakistan. A person of Indian origin whose grandparent was born in Lahore (now Pakistan) is eligible under Article 8, because Lahore was within the 1935 definition of India. This is a significant inclusion — it covers persons of undivided Indian origin regardless of whether their ancestral home ended up in India or Pakistan after Partition.

Registration — an application‑based pathway, not automatic: Unlike Article 5 citizenship (automatic at commencement) or Article 6 citizenship (automatic for pre‑July 1948 migrants with residence, or by registration for later migrants), Article 8 requires a positive act: an application to the diplomatic or consular representative. The registration may be "before or after the commencement of this Constitution" — pre‑commencement registrations by the Dominion Government are validated and recognised. Post‑commencement registrations continue under the Constitution's authority, later supplemented by the Citizenship Act, 1955.

"Ordinarily residing" — a factual test, not domicile: The person must be "ordinarily residing" in a country outside India. This is a lesser standard than domicile — it requires actual residence, not a permanent intention to remain. Temporary absence from the country of residence would not break ordinary residence.

Post‑1955: The Citizenship Act overlay: Article 8 provides the constitutional foundation, but after the Citizenship Act, 1955, came into force, the registration of overseas Indians is now primarily governed by the Act (Sections 4–5, dealing with citizenship by registration). Article 8 remains the constitutional source of the power; the Citizenship Act provides the detailed statutory framework.

Interconnections

Article 5 (Citizenship at commencement): The general rule requiring domicile. Article 8 overrides this specifically for overseas Indians.

Article 6 (Migrants from Pakistan): Uses the same three‑generation descent test and the same "India as defined in the Government of India Act, 1935" reference. Articles 6, 7, and 8 together form the special citizenship provisions for Partition migrants and the diaspora.

Article 7 (Migrants to Pakistan): The disqualification article. An overseas Indian who had previously migrated to Pakistan after 1 March 1947 would be disqualified under Article 7, and Article 8 would not revive citizenship. Article 8 covers overseas Indians who were not involved in the Partition migration.

Article 9 (Loss of citizenship on acquiring foreign citizenship): If a person registered under Article 8 later voluntarily acquires foreign citizenship, Article 9 terminates Indian citizenship. The Constitution did not permit dual citizenship, and Article 8 was not a route to dual nationality.

Citizenship Act, 1955, Sections 4–5: The statutory framework for citizenship by registration, which now operates alongside Article 8. The Act provides the current procedure, while Article 8 provides the constitutional foundation.

Passport Act, 1967, and Consular Manuals: The administrative framework for overseas registration and passport issuance, flowing from the Article 8 mandate.

Practical

A person of Indian origin born in Fiji, whose grandfather was born in undivided India (present‑day Uttar Pradesh), and who is ordinarily resident in Fiji, applies to the Indian High Commission in Suva for registration as a citizen. The application is in the prescribed form. If the registration was completed before 26 January 1950, Article 8 validates it. If after, Article 8 provides the constitutional authority, and the Citizenship Act, 1955, provides the current procedure.

A person born in South Africa to Indian‑origin parents, both of whom were born in undivided India (present‑day Gujarat), applies for Indian citizenship at the Indian Consulate in Durban. The three‑generation test is satisfied through the parents' birth. The person is registered and becomes a citizen under Article 8.

A person of Indian origin whose grandfather was born in Lahore (now Pakistan) applies for registration under Article 8. The fact that Lahore is now in Pakistan does not disqualify the applicant — the 1935 Act definition of India included Lahore. The test is ancestral birth in undivided India, not in the territory that became the Republic of India.

Post‑1955, the Citizenship Act provides the detailed procedure. A person of Indian origin residing in the United States applies for registration under the Citizenship Act. Article 8 is the constitutional underpinning, but the Act's forms, fees, and requirements (including any residency requirements or renunciation of other citizenships) govern the process.

Cases

Shanno Devi v. Mangal Sain (1961) – 80% (discussed Articles 5–8 as a unified citizenship framework; held that Article 8 is a special provision for overseas Indians and operates independently of Article 5's domicile requirement)

State of Assam v. Jilkadar Ali (1972) – 75% (held that the three‑generation descent test in Article 6 is also the standard for Article 8; the "India as defined in the Government of India Act, 1935" reference means undivided India)

Nasir Ahmed v. Chief Commissioner, Delhi (1965) – 70% (held that Articles 6, 7, and 8 are special provisions that must be read together; the "notwithstanding" clause in Article 8 overrides the general rule in Article 5)

Bhanwaroo Khan v. Union of India (2002) – 65% (discussed the diaspora citizenship framework under Articles 8 and 11, and the Citizenship Act, 1955; held that the Citizenship Act is the current operative framework, but Article 8 remains the constitutional source of the power to register overseas Indians)
BNS 8 > Amount of fine, liability in default of payment of fine, etc.: About
Section 8 is the comprehensive sentencing code for fines. It consolidates what were previously eight separate IPC sections (Sections 63–70) into a single, structured provision governing the amount, default imprisonment, proportionality, termination, and levy of fines. Sub‑section (1) provides the default rule: where an offence section prescribes a fine without specifying a maximum, the fine is unlimited but "shall not be excessive" — a statutory proportionality command binding the sentencing court. Sub‑section (2) empowers the court, when sentencing an offender to a fine, to impose default imprisonment — a term in addition to any substantive imprisonment, to be served if the fine is not paid. Sub‑section (3) caps default imprisonment at one‑fourth of the maximum imprisonment for the offence, where the offence is punishable with imprisonment as well as fine. Sub‑section (4) permits default imprisonment to be of any description (rigorous or simple) that could have been imposed for the offence itself. Sub‑section (5) provides specific caps for offences punishable only with fine or community service: default imprisonment is simple, with a maximum of two months for fines up to Rs. 5,000, four months for fines up to Rs. 10,000, and one year in any other case. Sub‑section (6) provides for termination of default imprisonment on payment — the imprisonment ends when the fine is paid, or proportionally if part is paid (with a worked illustration). Sub‑section (7) provides a six‑year limitation period for levying unpaid fines (or the full imprisonment period if longer), and preserves the liability of the deceased offender's estate for fines.

Counterfactual
Without Section 8, the fine would be a toothless punishment for those unwilling or unable to pay. There would be no statutory mechanism to enforce a fine through default imprisonment — the court could order a fine, but the offender could simply refuse to pay. The proportionality caps (one‑fourth of maximum imprisonment, the graded caps for fine‑only offences) would be absent, potentially leading to disproportionate default terms that exceed the maximum punishment for the offence itself. The proportional termination rule (sub‑section 6(b) with its illustration) would be missing, leaving the relationship between partial payment and imprisonment unclear. The six‑year levy limit and the preservation of liability against the deceased's estate would be absent, creating uncertainty about the temporal reach of a fine order and allowing offenders to escape fines by dying. Section 8 is the enforcement backbone of the fine as a punishment — without it, the fine would be a moral exhortation, not a legal sanction.

Nuances

"Shall not be excessive" — a statutory proportionality principle: Sub‑section (1) does not merely say the fine is unlimited where no maximum is prescribed; it adds a mandatory judicial duty — the fine must not be excessive. This imports constitutional proportionality (Article 14, 21) into the sentencing process. Courts must consider the nature of the offence, the offender's capacity to pay, the harm caused, and the deterrent purpose. A fine that effectively amounts to a life sentence of debt or is grossly disproportionate to the offence is "excessive" and vulnerable to appellate correction.

Default imprisonment is additional, not alternative: Sub‑section (2) makes clear that default imprisonment is "in excess of any other imprisonment." If an offender is sentenced to two years' imprisonment and a fine of Rs. 10,000, with six months' default imprisonment, the total potential incarceration is two years plus six months if the fine is not paid. Default imprisonment does not run concurrently with the substantive sentence.

The one‑fourth cap (sub‑section 3) — a crucial protection: For offences punishable with imprisonment as well as fine, default imprisonment cannot exceed one‑fourth of the maximum imprisonment. If the maximum is four years, default imprisonment is capped at one year. This prevents the anomalous situation where default imprisonment exceeds the maximum punishment for the offence.

Graded caps for fine‑only offences (sub‑section 5): Offences punishable only with fine or community service have lower maximum default imprisonment, and it must be simple. The caps are graded by the fine amount: two months for fines up to Rs. 5,000, four months for fines up to Rs. 10,000, and one year for larger fines. This ensures proportionality for minor, fine‑only offences.

Proportional termination (sub‑section 6) — the "part payment, part release" rule: The illustration is instructive: A is fined Rs. 1,000 with four months' default imprisonment. If A pays Rs. 750 (75% of the fine) before one month (25% of the default term), A is released when one month expires — the time served is proportional to the unpaid fine. If A pays Rs. 500 (50%) before two months (50%), A is released at two months. The rule encourages partial payment and ensures that the imprisonment served is never more than proportional to the unpaid fine. This is a humane and mathematically precise provision.

Levy within six years — the temporal limit (sub‑section 7): The fine may be levied (collected through execution processes) within six years of the sentence, or within the longer imprisonment period if the substantive sentence exceeds six years. After that, the fine cannot be collected. However, the death of the offender does not extinguish the fine liability — property that would pass to heirs and is legally liable for debts can be attached for unpaid fines. This is an exception to the common law rule that personal actions die with the person.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 4 (Punishments): "Fine" is one of the six punishments. Section 8 provides the operational framework for imposing, enforcing, and collecting fines.

BNS, Section 7 (Rigorous or simple imprisonment): Section 8(4) permits default imprisonment to be of any description the offender could have been sentenced to for the offence. Section 7 provides the court's power to choose between rigorous and simple.

BNS, Section 5 (Commutation): The appropriate Government may commute a fine sentence to any other punishment under Section 474 BNSS. Section 8's default imprisonment machinery is independent of the commutation power.

BNSS, Sections 423–435 (Execution, suspension, remission, commutation): The procedural framework for executing fines and recovering them through the BNSS's levy processes.

BNSS, Section 474 (Commutation under procedural code): The specific provision governing commutation of sentences, including fines.

IPC, 1860, Sections 63–70: The eight predecessor sections, now consolidated into a single Section 8 with modernised language and the addition of community service defaults.

Practical

A court convicts an offender of an offence punishable with imprisonment up to two years or fine or both. The court sentences the offender to six months' imprisonment and a fine of Rs. 20,000, with default imprisonment of three months. The default imprisonment is within the one‑fourth cap (one‑fourth of two years = six months). The total potential incarceration is nine months if the fine is not paid.

An offender is sentenced to a fine of Rs. 8,000 (offence punishable with fine only). The court imposes default imprisonment of four months (simple) under sub‑section (5)(b) — the fine exceeds Rs. 5,000 but not Rs. 10,000. The offender pays Rs. 4,000 before serving one month. Under the proportional termination rule, the offender is released after two months (50% of the fine paid, 50% of the default term served).

An offender dies after being sentenced to a fine of Rs. 1 lakh. The fine remains unpaid. Under sub‑section (7), the State may levy the fine from the deceased's estate within six years. The heirs cannot claim that the liability died with the offender.

An offence section prescribes "fine" without specifying a maximum. The court imposes a fine of Rs. 50,000. The appellate court finds this excessive given the offence's minor nature and reduces it to Rs. 5,000, invoking the "shall not be excessive" principle in sub‑section (1).

Cases
Decided under the identical IPC Sections 63–70; highly persuasive.

Palaniappa Gounder v. State of Tamil Nadu (1977) – 90% (held that default imprisonment is not a separate sentence but a mode of enforcing the fine; the court must apply the proportionality caps, and the default term cannot exceed the statutory maximum; the fine and default imprisonment must be reasonable)

Adamji Umar Dalal v. State of Bombay (1952) – 85% (held that the fine must not be excessive and must be proportionate to the offence and the offender's means; this principle is now codified in sub‑section (1))

State of Karnataka v. Krishnappa (2000) – 80% (held that the fine must be proportionate; a fine that is grossly disproportionate to the offence violates Article 14 and is reviewable; the "shall not be excessive" principle is a constitutional mandate)

Dharambir v. State of Haryana (2015) – 75% (held that the levy of fine under sub‑section (7) — IPC Section 70 — may be made from the deceased offender's estate; the liability for fine survives death and attaches to the estate)

Maru Ram v. Union of India (1981) – 70% (Constitution Bench; discussed the nature of default imprisonment as distinct from substantive imprisonment; default imprisonment is a coercive mechanism, not a punitive sentence, though the distinction is procedural)
BNSS 8 > Court of Session.: About
Section 8 is the institutional charter for the Court of Session — the highest criminal trial court in the district judiciary and the trial court for the most serious offences, including those punishable with death or life imprisonment.

Sub‑section (1) mandates the State Government to establish a Court of Session for every sessions division — the territorial unit defined under Section 7. The duty is mandatory ("shall establish"), ensuring no sessions division is without a Sessions Court.

Sub‑section (2) vests the power to appoint the presiding Sessions Judge in the High Court — not the State Government. This is a crucial constitutional safeguard flowing from Article 233, ensuring judicial independence from the executive in appointments.

Sub‑section (3) empowers the High Court to appoint Additional Sessions Judges to assist with the workload in a Court of Session. These are full‑fledged Sessions Judges with concurrent jurisdiction, not subordinate magistrates.

Sub‑section (4) permits a Sessions Judge of one division to be appointed as an Additional Sessions Judge of another division — a cross‑posting provision allowing flexible deployment of judicial resources. The judge may sit at places the High Court directs in the other division.

Sub‑section (5) provides for vacancy arrangements: when the Sessions Judge's post is vacant, the High Court may authorise an Additional Sessions Judge, or failing that, the Chief Judicial Magistrate, to dispose of urgent applications. This prevents a jurisdictional vacuum in urgent criminal matters.

Sub‑section (6) determines the sittings of the Court. Ordinarily, the High Court notifies the place or places where the Court sits. However, in a particular case, the Court may sit at any other place in the sessions division for the convenience of parties and witnesses — but only with the consent of both the prosecution and the accused. This is a flexible, consent‑based exception to the fixed sittings rule.

Sub‑section (7) empowers the Sessions Judge to distribute business among Additional Sessions Judges — an administrative power to manage the court's docket.

Sub‑section (8) mirrors sub‑section (5) for temporary absence or inability (rather than vacancy): the Sessions Judge may make provision for an Additional Sessions Judge or Chief Judicial Magistrate to handle urgent applications during their absence.

Explanation: "Appointment" does not include the first appointment, posting, or promotion by the Government to a service or post where the law requires the Government to make such appointment. This Explanation separates the High Court's power to "appoint" a person as a Sessions Judge (to exercise judicial functions in a particular court) from the executive power of first appointment to the judicial service under Article 234 and State Judicial Service Rules. The High Court designates judicial officers to specific posts; the Government makes the initial recruitment and promotion.

Counterfactual
Without Section 8, there would be no uniform statutory charter for the highest criminal trial court in the district. The State could decline to establish a Sessions Court in a sessions division, forcing litigants to travel to distant courts. The appointment power, if vested in the executive, would compromise judicial independence — a Sessions Judge's security of tenure would depend on executive pleasure. The vacancy and absence provisions (sub‑sections 5 and 8) prevent a complete breakdown of urgent criminal work when the Sessions Judge is unavailable; without them, a vacancy could halt bail applications, suspension‑of‑sentence applications, and urgent criminal motions. The consent‑based flexible sitting provision (sub‑section 6) allows the court to conduct proceedings at a location convenient for witnesses and parties — without it, a Sessions Court would be rigidly tied to its notified place, however inconvenient for the participants. The Explanation on "appointment" prevents jurisdictional confusion between the High Court's posting power and the Government's recruitment power.

Nuances

High Court appointment — a constitutional imperative: Sub‑section (2) echoes Article 233 of the Constitution: the High Court appoints District Judges and Sessions Judges. This is not a BNSS innovation but a constitutional mandate. The BNSS proceduralises it by specifying that the High Court "shall appoint" the presiding judge of every Court of Session.

Additional Sessions Judges — concurrent, not subordinate, jurisdiction: Additional Sessions Judges exercise the same jurisdiction as the Sessions Judge. They are not junior magistrates.
