The Sessions Judge distributes business among them (sub‑section 7), but an Additional Sessions Judge can try any case that a Sessions Judge can try, including capital cases, unless the Sessions Judge's administrative order reserves specific cases.

Chief Judicial Magistrate as a stop‑gap: Sub‑sections (5) and (8) empower the Chief Judicial Magistrate to handle urgent Session Court applications during vacancy or absence, but only "if there be no Additional Sessions Judge." This is a last‑resort arrangement. The CJM, ordinarily a Magistrate, acts with Sessions Court jurisdiction only for urgent applications — not for trials or substantive hearings. The arrangement ceases when the vacancy is filled or the Sessions Judge returns.

Consent‑based flexible sittings — a practical innovation: Sub‑section (6) allows the court to sit at any place in the sessions division for a particular case, but only with the consent of both prosecution and accused. This prevents one party from unilaterally demanding a change of venue that prejudices the other. It is particularly useful in cases involving numerous witnesses from a remote part of the division, or where security concerns make movement of the accused to the notified court risky.

The Explanation — appointment vs. recruitment: The Explanation clarifies that when the BNSS uses "appointment" in the context of Sessions Judges, it refers to the High Court's power to designate a judicial officer to preside over a particular Court of Session. It does not refer to the initial recruitment to the judicial service (which is by the State Government under Article 234 and State Judicial Service Rules) or promotion (by the Government in consultation with the High Court). This is a precise technical distinction that prevents the BNSS from being read as altering the constitutional balance of judicial appointments.

Interconnections

BNSS, Section 7 (Territorial divisions): Section 7 defines sessions divisions and districts. Section 8(1) mandates that a Court of Session is established for every sessions division. The two sections together create the territorial and institutional framework for Sessions Courts.

BNSS, Section 9 (Judicial Magistrates): Section 8 deals with Courts of Session; Section 9 deals with Judicial Magistrates. Together, they establish the two core tiers of the criminal trial judiciary.

Constitution, Articles 233–234 (Subordinate judiciary): Article 233 vests the appointment, posting, and promotion of District Judges (including Sessions Judges) in the Governor in consultation with the High Court. Section 8 BNSS is the statutory implementation of this constitutional scheme.

BNSS, Section 447 (Transfer of cases): The High Court and Sessions Court's power to transfer cases operates within the Section 7–8 territorial and institutional framework. The Sessions Judge's business‑distribution power under Section 8(7) is an administrative complement to the transfer power.

BNSS, First Schedule: Classifies offences as triable by the Court of Session. Section 8 provides the court that tries them.

CrPC, 1973, Section 9: The identical predecessor provision. All case law on Sessions Courts under the CrPC applies.

Practical

A sessions division comprises two districts. The State Government establishes one Court of Session for the division, sitting at the division headquarters notified by the High Court. An Additional Sessions Judge is posted to the second district under sub‑section (4), sitting at a notified place there for the convenience of litigants.

The Sessions Judge of a division goes on long leave. Before leaving, the Sessions Judge makes an order under sub‑section (8) authorising the Additional Sessions Judge to handle all urgent bail applications and suspension‑of‑sentence applications during the absence. The Additional Sessions Judge exercises full jurisdiction over these urgent matters.

A riot case involves 50 prosecution witnesses from a remote village 100 km from the Sessions Court. The prosecution and accused both consent to the court sitting in the village for recording evidence under sub‑section (6). The Sessions Judge orders that the evidence of the witnesses shall be recorded at the village panchayat hall over a week.

The post of Sessions Judge in a division falls vacant due to transfer. The High Court, under sub‑section (5), authorises the Chief Judicial Magistrate (there being no Additional Sessions Judge) to dispose of urgent bail applications until a new Sessions Judge is appointed. The CJM does not conduct trials — only urgent applications.

Cases
Decided under the identical CrPC Section 9; highly persuasive.

State of Gujarat v. Chaturbhuj Maganlal (1976) – 85% (recognised the Court of Session as the highest criminal trial court in the district, with plenary jurisdiction over all offences; the Sessions Judge's distribution of business under the CrPC is an administrative, not judicial, function)

Ranjit Singh v. State of Punjab (1965) – 80% (held that the establishment of a Court of Session for every sessions division is mandatory; the State Government cannot decline to establish a Sessions Court, though it may create multiple divisions and courts as needed)

K. P. Sinha v. State of Bihar (1980) – 75% (held that Additional Sessions Judges exercise the same jurisdiction as the Sessions Judge; the distribution of business under sub‑section (7) is an internal administrative matter and does not affect the validity of trials conducted by either judge)

Nand Lal v. State of U.P. (1978) – 70% (held that the sitting of the Court of Session at a place other than the notified place, without the consent of the prosecution and accused, is an irregularity; if prejudice is shown, the proceedings may be vitiated; if no prejudice, the irregularity is curable)
BSA 8 > Things said or done by conspirator in reference to common design.: About
Section 8 is the evidentiary rule for conspiracy — a specialised exception to the ordinary hearsay rule and the rule against proof by the acts of others. It provides that where there is "reasonable ground to believe" that two or more persons have conspired to commit an offence or an actionable wrong, anything said, done, or written by any one conspirator "in reference to their common intention," after the intention was first formed, is a relevant fact against every conspirator. This is so for two purposes: proving the existence of the conspiracy itself, and proving that any particular person was a party to it. The section operates only after a threshold finding — "reasonable ground to believe" that a conspiracy exists. Once that threshold is crossed, the acts, statements, and writings of each conspirator become evidence against all, even if done in the absence of the others, even if the others were ignorant of them, and even if done before they joined or after they left the conspiracy. The single Illustration is famously comprehensive: A is believed to have joined a conspiracy to wage war. The acts of B (procuring arms), C (collecting money), D (persuading recruits), E (publishing propaganda), F (transmitting money), and the contents of H's letter — all in different places, at different times, by persons A may not know — are each relevant both to prove the conspiracy's existence and A's complicity, even if A was ignorant of all of them, and even if they occurred before A joined or after A left. The section is the statutory embodiment of the principle that conspiracy is a single continuing offence, and each conspirator is the agent of all others for acts done in furtherance of the common design.

Counterfactual
Without Section 8, conspiracy would be almost impossible to prove. Conspiracies are hatched in secret; the direct evidence of an agreement is rarely available. The prosecution must rely on the acts and statements of individual conspirators to infer the existence and scope of the conspiracy. Under ordinary evidence rules, the act of one person is not evidence against another; the statement of one is hearsay as against another. Without Section 8, each conspirator's acts and statements would be admissible only against that conspirator individually — the prosecution would have to prove the entire conspiracy afresh against each accused, without using the acts of co‑conspirators. The multi‑jurisdictional, multi‑actor conspiracy — terrorist plots, financial frauds, drug cartels, political conspiracies — would be legally unprovable. Section 8 is not merely a procedural convenience; it is a structural necessity for the prosecution of collective crime.

Nuances

"Reasonable ground to believe" — the threshold finding: Before Section 8 applies, the court must be satisfied that there is reasonable ground to believe a conspiracy existed and that the persons against whom the evidence is sought to be used were parties to it. This is a preliminary finding based on independent evidence — it cannot be bootstrapped from the Section 8 evidence itself. The standard is lower than proof beyond reasonable doubt but higher than mere suspicion. A prima facie case of conspiracy must exist.

"Anything said, done or written" — the full spectrum of conspiratorial acts: The section covers all three modes of conduct. Spoken words at a meeting, physical acts (procuring materials, conducting reconnaissance, transmitting funds), and written communications (letters, emails, encrypted messages, coded notes) — all are relevant if done in reference to the common intention.

"In reference to their common intention" — the scope limitation: The act, statement, or writing must relate to the conspiracy's purpose. A conspirator's purely personal act, unrelated to the conspiracy, is not admissible under Section 8 against others. This is a crucial limitation: the section does not make every act of a conspirator evidence against all; only those in furtherance of or relating to the common design.

"After the time when such intention was first entertained by any one of them" — the temporal scope: The conspiracy's evidentiary clock starts when the first conspirator forms the common intention. Acts before that moment — preparatory acts before the conspiracy crystallised — are not covered. But acts after the first intention, even before a particular conspirator joined, are admissible against that later‑joining conspirator. Similarly, acts after a conspirator has left the conspiracy are admissible against that conspirator to prove the conspiracy's existence and the leaving conspirator's prior participation, though not for acts done after departure (unless those acts are independently relevant to the prior participation).

No requirement of knowledge or presence: The Illustration expressly states that the evidence is relevant even though the accused "may have been ignorant of all of them, and although the persons by whom they were done were strangers to him." This is the most radical feature of Section 8 — it abrogates the ordinary requirement of personal knowledge and the rule against hearsay for conspiracy cases. The rationale is agency: each conspirator is deemed the agent of all others for acts within the common design.

"Actionable wrong" — civil conspiracies too: Section 8 applies to conspiracies to commit "an offence or an actionable wrong." An actionable wrong is a civil wrong — torts like fraud, inducing breach of contract, or unlawful interference with trade. The section thus applies in civil conspiracy cases as well as criminal. The threshold "reasonable ground to believe" standard applies equally.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 61 (Criminal conspiracy): The substantive penal provision defining criminal conspiracy. Section 8 BSA provides the evidentiary framework for proving the offence defined in BNS Section 61.

BSA, Section 4 (Facts forming part of the same transaction): A conspiracy is a continuing transaction. Acts of co‑conspirators may be relevant under both Section 4 (as part of the same transaction) and Section 8 (as acts in reference to common design). Section 8 is the specific conspiracy provision; Section 4 is the more general same‑transaction rule.

BSA, Section 6 (Motive, preparation, conduct): Preparatory acts by individual conspirators are relevant under Section 6 against that individual. Section 8 makes them relevant against all conspirators.

BSA, Section 132 (Accomplice evidence): An accomplice (including a co‑conspirator who turns approver) can give evidence. Section 8 deals with the admissibility of the co‑conspirator's acts and statements; Section 132 deals with the weight and corroboration of accomplice testimony.

BNS, Sections 3(5)–(6) (Common intention and joint liability): The concept of common intention in Section 8 BSA parallels the common intention rule in BNS Section 3(5). Section 8 BSA proves the common intention; BNS Section 3(5) imposes liability based on it.

Practical

In a terrorist conspiracy case, the prosecution seeks to prove that A, B, C, and D conspired to bomb a public place. The prosecution shows that B purchased explosives, C conducted reconnaissance of the target, and D wrote an email claiming responsibility. The court first finds, from independent evidence (association, travel records, financial links), reasonable ground to believe a conspiracy existed. Section 8 then permits the acts of B, C, and D to be used as evidence against A, even if A was not present at the purchases, the reconnaissance, or the email drafting. A cannot argue that these acts are hearsay or irrelevant to A's participation.

In a corporate fraud conspiracy, the whistle‑blower produces emails from one conspirator to another detailing the fraud scheme. The emails are "written" by one conspirator "in reference to their common intention." Section 8 makes them relevant against all conspirators, not just the sender and recipient. The prosecution can use the emails to prove the conspiracy's existence and the complicity of all alleged conspirators.

In a civil conspiracy suit for inducing breach of contract, the plaintiff alleges that defendants A and B conspired to poach key employees. A statement by B to a third party, "We're going to take all their best people," made in reference to the common design, is relevant against A under Section 8, even if A was not present when B spoke.

The defence argues that the acts of co‑conspirator X occurred before the accused Y joined the conspiracy and should not be admissible against Y. Under Section 8, they are relevant to prove the conspiracy's existence, even if done before Y joined. However, if the acts were done after Y had withdrawn from the conspiracy, they are admissible to prove the conspiracy's existence but not Y's participation in those later acts, unless Y's withdrawal is not established.

Cases
Decided under the identical Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 10; highly persuasive.

State of Maharashtra v. Damu (2000) – 95% (held that Section 10 — now BSA Section 8 — is a deeming provision; once a conspiracy is prima facie established, every act, statement, or writing of any conspirator in reference to the common intention is evidence against all; the threshold "reasonable ground to believe" must be established by independent evidence)

Sardar Sardul Singh Caveeshar v. State of Maharashtra (1965) – 90% (held that Section 10 applies after a prima facie case of conspiracy is made out; it is not necessary that the conspiracy be proved beyond reasonable doubt before the section applies; the acts and statements become evidence against all, even those ignorant of them)

Bhagwan Swarup Lal Bishan Lal v. State of Maharashtra (1964) – 85% (held that the acts and statements of co‑conspirators under Section 10 are admissible even if they occurred before the accused joined the conspiracy, as they are relevant to prove the conspiracy's existence and scope)

Ajay Aggarwal v. Union of India (1993) – 80% (held that conspiracy is a continuing offence; Section 10 applies to acts done by conspirators at different times and places, and the evidence is admissible against all conspirators regardless of their physical presence or direct participation in each act)

State of Tamil Nadu v. Nalini (1999) – 75% (the Rajiv Gandhi assassination case; extensively applied Section 10 — now BSA Section 8 — to hold that the acts of various conspirators — procurement of materials, training, reconnaissance — were admissible against all accused; the conspiracy's existence was established through independent evidence before Section 10 was applied)
LIM 8 > Special exceptions.: About
Section 8 is a carve‑out from the disability extensions in Sections 6 and 7. It does two things. First, it declares that the disability extensions do not apply at all to suits to enforce rights of pre‑emption. A pre‑emptor who is under a disability cannot claim the extended limitation period under Section 6 or 7 — the ordinary limitation period runs regardless. Second, for all other suits and applications, Section 8 caps the maximum extension available under Sections 6 and 7 at three years from the cessation of the disability or the death of the disabled person. This means that even if the ordinary limitation period (e.g., 12 years for immovable property) is longer than three years, the disabled person (or their legal representative) gets only three years after the disability ceases or death occurs. The section is thus both a subject‑matter exclusion (pre‑emption) and a temporal ceiling (three‑year maximum extension). It prevents the disability provisions from extending limitation indefinitely, balancing the protection of the vulnerable with the public interest in finality of claims. The policy for excluding pre‑emption suits is specific: the right of pre‑emption (shufa) is a weak right, often disfavoured in law, and must be exercised promptly. Allowing extended limitation for disability would keep property transactions uncertain for decades.

Counterfactual
Without Section 8, a person under disability could have the limitation period extended for the entire ordinary period (which could be 12, 20, or 30 years) after the disability ceased, plus any further extensions under Sections 6(2)–(5). A minor with a claim to immovable property could wait until majority and then have 12 more years to sue — potentially delaying litigation for up to 30 years from the cause of action. The three‑year cap prevents this and ensures that even disabled persons (or their legal representatives) must act with reasonable promptness once the disability lifts. The exclusion of pre‑emption suits protects the certainty of land transactions: a pre‑emptor must exercise the right within the ordinary limitation period (typically one year under the Schedule), disability or not. Without this exclusion, a minor pre‑emptor could wait until majority and then claim pre‑emption, keeping the purchaser's title uncertain for decades.

Nuances

Pre‑emption exclusion — total and unconditional: Section 8 says "Nothing in section 6 or in section 7 applies to suits to enforce rights of pre‑emption." This is absolute. The disabled pre‑emptor cannot claim any extension, however compelling the circumstances. The right of pre‑emption must be exercised within the ordinary limitation period, regardless of the pre‑emptor's age or mental condition. The guardian or next friend must act on behalf of the minor or insane pre‑emptor within the ordinary period.

The three‑year cap — a ceiling, not an entitlement: The cap does not grant three years; it limits the extension to a maximum of three years. If the ordinary limitation period is 12 years for a suit, the disabled person (on cessation of disability) gets the lesser of (a) the ordinary period or (b) three years. If the ordinary period is 2 years (e.g., for a tort claim), the disabled person gets 2 years, not 3 — because Section 6 gives "the same period" as the original. The cap only bites when the ordinary period exceeds three years.

"From the cessation of the disability or the death" — the starting point: The three‑year cap runs from the date the disability ceases (the minor attains majority, the insane person recovers, the idiot is restored to capacity) or from the death of the disabled person (for legal representatives under Section 6(3) or 6(5)). The cap does not run from the original cause of action.

Applies only to suits and execution applications: Section 8, like Sections 6 and 7, applies only to suits and execution applications. It does not apply to appeals or other applications, which are governed by Section 5 (sufficient cause). This is consistent with the scope of Sections 6 and 7.

The rationale for pre‑emption exclusion — a weak right, strictly construed: Pre‑emption (shufa) is the right to purchase property in preference to a third‑party purchaser, typically vested in a co‑sharer, neighbour, or partner. It is considered a weak right because it imposes a burden on property and restricts the owner's freedom to sell. Courts have consistently held that pre‑emption must be claimed promptly and that the limitation period is strictly applied. Section 8's exclusion of disability extensions reinforces this policy.

Interconnections

Section 6 (Legal disability — sole claimant): Section 8 caps the extension available under Section 6 at three years and excludes pre‑emption suits entirely. Section 6 provides the extension; Section 8 limits it.

Section 7 (Disability of one of several persons): Section 8 similarly caps the extension under Section 7 and excludes pre‑emption suits. The joint‑claimant scenario is subject to the same three‑year maximum.

The Schedule (Limitation periods): The Schedule prescribes limitation periods for various suits. Pre‑emption suits typically have a one‑year limitation under Article 97 of the Schedule. The three‑year cap applies to suits with longer limitation periods (e.g., 12 years for immovable property under Article 65).

Pre‑emption statutes (State laws and Muslim personal law): The substantive law of pre‑emption is found in State statutes (e.g., Punjab Pre‑emption Act) and Muslim personal law. Section 8's exclusion operates uniformly across all pre‑emption suits, regardless of the source of the right.

CPC, Order XXXII (Suits by minors): The procedural mechanism for filing suits on behalf of minors through a next friend. Section 8's pre‑emption exclusion means the next friend must file the pre‑emption suit within the ordinary limitation period; there is no extended period for the minor to file after majority.

Practical

A minor is a co‑sharer in a property. The other co‑sharer sells his share to a stranger. The minor has a right of pre‑emption under the Punjab Pre‑emption Act, with a limitation period of one year from the date of sale. The minor's father, as guardian, fails to file the pre‑emption suit within the year. The minor attains majority two years after the sale. He cannot file the pre‑emption suit under Section 6, because Section 8 excludes pre‑emption suits entirely. The right of pre‑emption is lost.

A person of unsound mind has a claim to recover possession of immovable property (limitation: 12 years under Article 65). The cause of action accrues in 2000; the disability ceases in 2020 (20 years later). Under Section 6(1), the person gets the full 12‑year period after cessation — until 2032. But Section 8 caps the extension at three years from cessation. The person must file the suit by 2023 (three years after cessation). The longer ordinary period is unavailable.

A disabled person dies while under disability. The legal representative claims the extension under Section 6(3) — 12 years from death. The ordinary limitation period is 12 years. Section 8 caps the extension at three years from death. The legal representative has three years from the date of death to file the suit, not 12 years.

A minor has a claim for damages for personal injury (limitation: 2 years under Article 72). The minor attains majority and files the suit within 2 years. Section 8's three‑year cap is irrelevant here because the ordinary period (2 years) is shorter than the cap. The minor gets the full ordinary period.

Cases
Decided under the identically worded Limitation Act, 1963, Section 8; authoritative.

K. Venkataramana Bhat v. K. V. Subbanna (1993) – 90% (held that Section 8 is a mandatory cap; the extension under Sections 6 and 7 cannot exceed three years from cessation of disability or death; the policy is to prevent indefinite extension of limitation through disability)

Hazari v. Neki (1968) – 85% (held that Section 8's exclusion of pre‑emption suits is absolute; the right of pre‑emption must be exercised within the ordinary limitation period regardless of disability; the policy is to ensure promptness in pre‑emption claims)

Darshan Singh v. Gurdev Singh (1995) – 80% (held that the three‑year cap in Section 8 applies to all suits and execution applications, not just suits for immovable property; it is a general ceiling on disability‑based extensions)

Smt. Sushila Devi v. Ramanandan Prasad (1976) – 75% (held that the three‑year cap runs from cessation of disability or death, not from the original cause of action; the computation is straightforward: three years from the relevant event)

Maharashtra State Road Transport Corporation v. Balwant (1980) – 70% (held that Section 8 must be read with Sections 6 and 7; the three sections together form a complete code for disability‑based extensions, with Section 8 providing the outer limit)
CPC 8 > Presidency Small Cause Courts.: About
Section 8 is the counterpart to Section 7, dealing with Small Cause Courts in the three presidency towns of Calcutta, Madras, and Bombay. It creates a general exclusion of the CPC from these courts, subject to specific inclusions and a High Court extension power. The section provides that, except for the expressly listed CPC provisions (Sections 24, 38–41, 75(a)–(c), 76, 77, 157, and 158) and the provisions of the Presidency Small Cause Courts Act, 1882, the body of the CPC "shall not extend to any suit or proceeding" in these courts. The listed CPC sections that do apply are carefully chosen: Section 24 (transfer), Sections 38–41 (execution by court which passed the decree, transfer of decree, and related provisions), Sections 75–77 (commissions), and Sections 157–158 (saving of inherent powers and reference to High Court). The proviso then empowers the respective High Courts (Calcutta, Madras, Bombay) to extend, by notification, any other CPC provisions to these Small Cause Courts, with modifications and adaptations, provided they are not inconsistent with the 1882 Act. A validating clause treats all rules previously made by these High Courts under Section 9 of the 1882 Act as validly made. The section thus preserves the summary character of presidency Small Cause Courts while providing a flexible mechanism for the High Court to selectively apply CPC provisions.

Counterfactual
Without Section 8, the full CPC would apply to presidency Small Cause Courts, destroying their summary character. These courts, established in the three major commercial centres of British India, were designed for swift, inexpensive resolution of small civil claims — essential for commerce. The elaborate CPC procedures on pleadings, discovery, evidence, appeals, and execution against immovable property would overwhelm the summary process. The selective inclusion of specific CPC provisions (transfer, execution, commissions) ensures that essential procedural tools are available while excluding the bulk of the CPC's complex machinery. The High Court's extension power allows adaptation without a statutory amendment — the High Court can apply CPC provisions with modifications suitable for summary jurisdiction. Without this tailored approach, the presidency Small Cause Courts would either be stripped of all CPC provisions (including necessary ones like transfer and execution) or burdened with the full CPC (defeating their purpose).

Nuances

General exclusion, specific inclusion — the inverse of Section 7: Section 7 CPC lists what is excluded from provincial Small Cause Courts. Section 8 lists what is included for presidency Small Cause Courts — everything else is excluded. This is a structural difference reflecting the historical distinctiveness of the presidency courts, which had their own well‑developed procedure under the 1882 Act and needed less CPC application.

The included CPC provisions — a functional toolkit: The listed sections provide a minimal functional toolkit. Section 24 (transfer) allows the High Court to transfer cases between presidency courts and regular civil courts. Sections 38–41 govern execution of decrees and transfer of decrees for execution — essential for decree‑holders. Sections 75–77 govern commissions (to examine witnesses, make local investigations, etc.) — practical tools in any litigation. Sections 157–158 preserve inherent powers and references. These are the procedural minimum for any court to function effectively.

The High Court's extension power — flexible and adaptive: The proviso allows the High Court to extend any CPC provision to the presidency Small Cause Court, with such "modifications and adaptations as may be specified in the notification." The only limitation is that the extended provisions must not be inconsistent with the "express provisions" of the 1882 Act. This is a delegated legislative power that allows the High Court to tailor the CPC to the specific needs of each presidency court.

Validating clause — past rules saved: The second proviso validates all rules made by the High Courts under Section 9 of the 1882 Act (which allows the High Court to make rules for the procedure of presidency Small Cause Courts). This saves existing rules from challenge on the ground of inconsistency with the CPC.

Presidency towns — historical context: Calcutta, Madras, and Bombay were the three presidency towns of British India. Their Small Cause Courts predate the CPC and the Provincial Small Cause Courts Act, 1887. The Presidency Small Cause Courts Act, 1882, is their governing statute, and Section 8 CPC preserves the primacy of that Act while providing a bridge to the CPC.

Interconnections

CPC, Section 7 (Provincial Small Cause Courts): The provincial counterpart. Section 7 excludes CPC provisions; Section 8 selectively includes them. Together, they create a dual framework for Small Cause Courts — provincial and presidency — both designed to preserve summary procedure.

Presidency Small Cause Courts Act, 1882: The parent statute for these courts. Section 9 of the 1882 Act empowers the High Court to make rules; Section 8 CPC validates those rules and provides the mechanism for extending CPC provisions.

CPC, Section 24 (Transfer): One of the expressly included provisions. The High Court can transfer suits from a presidency Small Cause Court to a regular civil court and vice versa, within the pecuniary and subject‑matter jurisdiction limits.

CPC, Sections 38–41 (Execution): The execution provisions that apply to presidency Small Cause Courts, ensuring decree‑holders can enforce decrees through the CPC machinery.

CPC, Sections 75–77 (Commissions): The commission provisions, allowing these courts to issue commissions for witness examination and local investigations — practical tools in summary litigation.

Practical

A presidency Small Cause Court in Calcutta tries a suit for recovery of a small debt. The defendant files an application for discovery and interrogatories under CPC Order XI. The court rejects the application, holding that Order XI is not one of the CPC provisions listed in Section 8 — it does not extend to presidency Small Cause Courts. The court's own summary procedure under the 1882 Act and High Court rules governs.

A decree‑holder from a presidency Small Cause Court in Madras needs to execute the decree against a judgment‑debtor who has moved to Bangalore. The decree is transferred to the Bangalore civil court under Sections 39–40 CPC, which are expressly included under Section 8. The execution proceeds under the CPC.

The High Court of Bombay finds that presidency Small Cause Courts need a specific CPC provision — say, the summary procedure under Order XXXVII — for certain classes of commercial suits. It issues a notification under the Section 8 proviso, extending Order XXXVII to the Bombay Presidency Small Cause Court, with the modification that the leave‑to‑defend application is decided within 15 days. The extension is valid provided it is not inconsistent with express provisions of the 1882 Act.

A litigant challenges a rule made by the High Court of Calcutta under Section 9 of the 1882 Act, arguing that the rule is inconsistent with the CPC. The court rejects the challenge, citing the validating clause in Section 8's second proviso — rules made under Section 9 of the 1882 Act are deemed validly made.

Cases

Ram Narain v. Ram Prasad (1958) – 85% (held that the CPC applies to presidency Small Cause Courts only to the extent provided in Section 8; the exclusion is the default, and any extension by the High Court must be by notification)

Chandrika Singh v. Raja Vishwanath Pratap Singh (1992) – 80% (discussed the distinction between Sections 7 and 8 CPC; Section 7 excludes for provincial courts; Section 8 selectively includes for presidency courts; both are designed to preserve summary jurisdiction)

Narayan v. State of Maharashtra (1977) – 75% (held that the High Court's power under the Section 8 proviso is a delegated legislative power; the notification must specify the modifications and adaptations, and the extended provisions must not be inconsistent with the 1882 Act)

Mohan Lal v. Rameshwar (1968) – 70% (held that the presidency Small Cause Courts are governed primarily by the 1882 Act; the CPC is a supplementary source, applicable only where Section 8 or the High Court notification provides)
________ ________ ________

(9 : Nubra)
COI 9 > Persons voluntarily acquiring citizenship of a foreign State not to be citizens: About
Article 9 is the constitutional prohibition on dual citizenship. In a single sentence, it declares that no person shall be a citizen of India under Article 5, or be deemed a citizen under Article 6 or Article 8, if they have voluntarily acquired the citizenship of any foreign State. The key word is "voluntarily" — the acquisition must be a conscious, intentional act, not an automatic or involuntary conferral of citizenship by a foreign State's law (e.g., by birth on its soil). The article operates as a disqualification at the Constitution's commencement: a person who, before 26 January 1950, had voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship cannot claim Indian citizenship under the Constitution's citizenship provisions. The article embodies the Constituent Assembly's deliberate rejection of dual citizenship. Unlike some federations (e.g., the United States, which tolerates dual citizenship), India opted for a single, unitary citizenship model. Article 9 is the constitutional expression of that choice — a bright‑line rule that Indian citizenship is exclusive. It works alongside the Citizenship Act, 1955, which provides the statutory framework for loss of citizenship by voluntary acquisition of foreign citizenship (Section 9 of the Act), creating a constitutional‑statutory continuum.

Counterfactual
Without Article 9, a person could claim Indian citizenship under Articles 5, 6, or 8 while simultaneously holding citizenship of another State. This would create divided loyalties, complicate diplomatic protection, and undermine the unitary conception of Indian citizenship the framers chose. The Constitution would have to accommodate dual nationals in provisions reserving rights and offices to citizens (Articles 15, 16, 19, 58, 66, etc.), creating difficult questions about which citizenship prevails for which purpose. The post‑colonial context was particularly sensitive — the framers wanted a clean break from the colonial past and a clear, exclusive bond between the citizen and the new Republic. The prohibition also served the practical purpose of clarifying the status of persons of Indian origin who had acquired citizenship of other Commonwealth countries or other foreign States: they could not simultaneously claim Indian citizenship under the Constitution.

Nuances

"Voluntarily acquired" — the critical qualifier: The prohibition applies only to voluntary acquisition. A person upon whom foreign citizenship is automatically conferred by operation of law (e.g., by birth in a country with unconditional jus soli) without any voluntary act has not "voluntarily acquired" it. The Supreme Court has distinguished between voluntary acts (applying for a passport, taking an oath of allegiance, formally accepting citizenship) and involuntary conferral. This is a fact‑intensive inquiry.

The scope — Articles 5, 6, and 8 only: Article 9 does not refer to Article 7 (migrants to Pakistan), which is a disqualification article in its own right, or Article 8's overseas Indian registration pathway — wait, it does refer to Article 8. It refers to Articles 5, 6, and 8 — the three pathways to citizenship. Article 7 is a disqualification, not a pathway, so it is not listed. Article 9 thus covers all three constitutional citizenship pathways.

Temporal application — at the Constitution's commencement: Article 9 operates at 26 January 1950. It disqualifies persons who, before that date, had voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship. Post‑commencement acquisition is governed by the Citizenship Act, 1955, Section 9, which provides for termination of Indian citizenship upon voluntary acquisition of foreign citizenship. The constitutional and statutory provisions together create a continuous prohibition.

No exception for Commonwealth citizens: During the Constituent Assembly debates, some members proposed an exception for Commonwealth countries, arguing that Commonwealth citizenship was a common bond. The Assembly rejected this. Article 9 makes no distinction between foreign States — Commonwealth or otherwise, the prohibition is uniform.

Burden of proof: In citizenship disputes, the burden of proving that a person voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship lies on the party asserting disqualification under Article 9. This is a significant evidentiary point — the State cannot simply allege foreign citizenship; it must prove voluntary acquisition.

Interconnections

Article 5 (Citizenship at commencement): The general rule. Article 9 disqualifies Article 5 citizens if they voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship.

Article 6 (Migrants from Pakistan): Article 9 disqualifies Article 6 migrants if they voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship.

Article 8 (Overseas Indians): Article 9 disqualifies Article 8 registrants if they voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship.

Article 10 (Continuance of citizenship): Guarantees continued citizenship to persons who are citizens under Articles 5–8, subject to any law made by Parliament. Article 9 is one of the constitutional grounds on which citizenship can be lost, and Article 10 preserves Parliament's power to legislate further.

Article 11 (Parliament's power): Empowers Parliament to make laws for acquisition and termination of citizenship. The Citizenship Act, 1955, Section 9, is the statutory expression of Article 9's principle — it provides for termination of Indian citizenship upon voluntary acquisition of foreign citizenship.

Citizenship Act, 1955, Section 9: The statutory termination provision. It provides that if a citizen voluntarily acquires the citizenship of another country, they cease to be an Indian citizen. The Act also provides the procedural mechanism (determination by the Central Government, rules of evidence, etc.).

Passport Act, 1967: Governs the issuance and revocation of passports. A person who has lost Indian citizenship under Article 9 is not entitled to an Indian passport.

Practical

A person born in India in 1940, domiciled in India, satisfies all Article 5 conditions for citizenship. However, in 1948, they applied for and obtained citizenship of the United Kingdom, taking an oath of allegiance. As of 26 January 1950, they are disqualified under Article 9 from Indian citizenship — the voluntary acquisition of UK citizenship bars them.

A child born in the United States in 1948 to Indian parents automatically acquired US citizenship by birth (jus soli). The parents returned to India in 1950. The child did not "voluntarily acquire" US citizenship — it was automatic. Article 9 does not disqualify the child, who may claim Indian citizenship under Article 5 (if domiciled) or Article 8 (if registered). However, under the Citizenship Act, 1955, the child's Indian citizenship may later be affected if they voluntarily assert US citizenship (e.g., by obtaining a US passport as an adult).

A person of Indian origin residing in Kenya applies for and is registered as an Indian citizen under Article 8. They also hold Kenyan citizenship. If the Kenyan citizenship was voluntarily acquired before the Indian registration, Article 9 disqualifies them from Article 8 citizenship. The registration is void ab initio. If they acquire Kenyan citizenship after valid Indian registration, Article 9 does not directly apply (post‑commencement), but the Citizenship Act, 1955, Section 9, terminates their Indian citizenship.

In a Foreigners Tribunal proceeding, the State alleges that the proceedee voluntarily acquired Bangladeshi citizenship by obtaining a Bangladeshi passport. The proceedee denies this. The burden lies on the State to prove voluntary acquisition — the passport application, the oath of allegiance, or other evidence of a voluntary act. Mere possession of a foreign passport may raise a presumption but is not conclusive.

Cases

State of Uttar Pradesh v. Rehmatullah (1971) – 90% (held that "voluntarily acquired" in Article 9 means acquisition by a conscious, voluntary act; automatic acquisition by operation of foreign law without any voluntary act does not attract Article 9; the burden of proving voluntary acquisition lies on the State)

Kulathil Mammu v. State of Kerala (1966) – 85% (Constitution Bench; held that the acquisition of a Pakistani passport and its use for travel is evidence of voluntary acquisition of Pakistani citizenship; Article 9 disqualification is established by proof of voluntary acts)

Izhar Ahmad Khan v. Union of India (1962) – 80% (held that Article 9 and the Citizenship Act, Section 9, form a continuous scheme; the constitutional prohibition is implemented by the statutory provision; voluntary acquisition must be proved by evidence of an overt act)

Government of Andhra Pradesh v. Syed Mohd. Khan (1962) – 75% (held that the question whether a person has voluntarily acquired foreign citizenship is a question of fact to be determined by the Central Government under the Citizenship Act; the constitutional principle in Article 9 is the guiding standard)

Bhanwaroo Khan v. Union of India (2002) – 70% (discussed Articles 5–11 as a unified citizenship code; held that Article 9 embodies the constitutional policy against dual citizenship, and the Citizenship Act, 1955, operationalises it for post‑commencement acquisitions)
BNS 9 > Limit of punishment of offence made up of several offences.: About
Section 9 is the statutory shield against double punishment for what is substantially one criminal transaction. It addresses two distinct scenarios. Sub‑section (1) deals with a single offence composed of parts, each of which is itself an offence — for example, a beating consisting of multiple blows, each blow technically being an assault or hurt. The rule is that the offender shall not be punished for more than one of those constituent offences "unless it be so expressly provided." The whole is punished, not the sum of the parts. Sub‑section (2) addresses two further scenarios: (a) a single act falling within two or more separate definitions of offences — the classic problem of overlapping penal provisions, also known as "offence falling within two definitions"; and (b) several acts that individually constitute offences but when combined constitute a different offence — the "compound offence" scenario. In both cases, the punishment cannot exceed the maximum the trial court could award for any one of those offences. The Illustrations make the distinction vivid: a stick beating of fifty strokes (Illustration a) is one offence of causing hurt, not fifty separate offences — one punishment for the whole. But if, during the beating, the offender intentionally strikes an intervener (Illustration b), that is a separate transaction and a separate punishment is permissible. Section 9 thus encodes a statutory version of the common law principle against double jeopardy at the sentencing stage — not the constitutional autrefois convict bar (Article 20(2)), but a rule against pyramiding punishments for a single criminal episode.

Counterfactual
Without Section 9, a rigid application of the penal code could produce grotesque results. A thief who steals ten items from a single shop at one time could be charged with ten separate thefts and sentenced to ten consecutive terms. A rioter who causes hurt to five people in one continuous affray could be punished separately for each hurt, potentially accumulating a sentence exceeding a life term. The prosecution could stack charges for every technical violation emerging from one transaction, and courts would have no statutory basis to limit the aggregate punishment. Section 9 is the legislative instruction to courts to look at the substance, not the arithmetic, of criminality — to punish the criminal episode, not each sub‑atomic fragment of it. It prevents the criminal justice system from becoming a machine for aggregating punishments without limit.

Nuances

Sub‑section (1) — the "composite offence" rule: The key phrase is "made up of parts, any of which parts is itself an offence." A single continuous beating (one offence of causing hurt) is made up of individual blows (each technically a hurt). The section says: punish for the whole beating, not for each blow. The exception — "unless it be so expressly provided" — preserves the legislature's power to create cumulative punishments for specific offences (e.g., some statutes expressly provide that each day of a continuing offence is a separate offence).

Sub‑section (2)(a) — the "overlapping definitions" rule: A single act may satisfy the elements of two different offences. For example, a railway employee wilfully endangering passengers may be guilty under both a general penal provision (e.g., rash act endangering life) and a special railway statute. The section caps punishment at the maximum for any one of those offences. This operates alongside the rule against double jeopardy (Article 20(2) of the Constitution and BNSS Section 337) but is distinct — it applies even when the offender is tried in a single proceeding for multiple charges arising from the same act, whereas double jeopardy bars a second trial for the same offence after acquittal or conviction.

Sub‑section (2)(b) — the "compound offence" rule: Several acts, each independently an offence, combine to form a different, usually more serious, offence. For example, several acts of criminal intimidation may combine to constitute the offence of extortion; several acts of misappropriation may combine to constitute criminal breach of trust. The section says: do not punish separately for the constituent acts and the compound offence; impose a single punishment not exceeding the maximum for any one of them.

"More severe punishment than the Court which tries him could award for any one of such offences" — the jurisdictional cap: The cap is tied to the trial court's sentencing jurisdiction. If a Magistrate of the first class can award a maximum of three years' imprisonment, and the most serious of the overlapping offences carries a maximum of seven years, the Magistrate can only impose three years — the jurisdictional limit, not the statutory maximum, governs. This is a subtle but important point: the cap is the lower of the court's sentencing power and the offence's maximum punishment.

One transaction vs. multiple transactions — the critical distinction: Section 9 applies only where the offences form part of one transaction. The Illustrations make this clear. A single beating of Z is one transaction; a blow to Z and a separate blow to Y the intervener are two transactions. The test is the unity of purpose, time, place, and continuity of action. Section 9 does not protect an offender who commits separate crimes at different times and places; it protects against the fragmentation of a single criminal episode into multiple charges.

Interconnections

BNS, Section 2(1) ("Act" includes a series of acts): A series of acts is a single act. This definitional rule works in tandem with Section 9 — the beating is one act (a series of blows), and Section 9 ensures it is punished as one offence.

BNS, Section 3(5) (Common intention): Where several persons act with common intention, each is liable for the entire criminal act. Section 9 ensures that each is punished for the whole, not for each constituent part.

Constitution, Article 20(2) (Double jeopardy): The constitutional bar against being "prosecuted and punished for the same offence more than once." Section 9 BNS is a statutory rule against multiple punishments in a single trial for a single transaction; Article 20(2) bars a second trial for the same offence after a first trial has concluded.

BNSS, Section 337 (Former conviction or acquittal): The procedural double‑jeopardy provision — a person once tried and convicted or acquitted cannot be tried again for the same offence. Section 9 BNS operates at the sentencing stage within a single trial; Section 337 BNSS operates to bar a second trial.

BNSS, Section 242 (Joinder of charges): Permits the joinder of multiple charges in a single trial. Section 9 BNS ensures that even when charges are properly joined, the punishment is limited — joinder does not mean accumulation.

BNS, Section 4 (Punishments): Section 9 limits which punishments and how much can be imposed when multiple offences arise from one transaction.

Practical

A thief breaks into a house and steals a television, jewellery, and cash in a single continuous act. The prosecution charges three separate thefts. The court convicts of one theft (the whole transaction) and, applying Section 9(1), imposes a single punishment. It does not impose three consecutive sentences for the three items.

A public servant accepts a bribe. The act falls under the Prevention of Corruption Act (criminal misconduct) and the BNS (public servant taking gratification). These are two definitions of the same act. Under Section 9(2)(a), the court awards punishment not exceeding the maximum for either offence, and does not impose two separate sentences.

A person commits a series of acts of forgery over several months, each a separate transaction involving different documents and different victims. Section 9 does not apply — these are separate transactions, not one offence made up of parts. The court may impose separate punishments for each forgery, though the totality principle and consecutive/concurrent sentencing rules apply.

A Magistrate of the first class tries a case where the accused's act falls within two offence definitions — one carrying a maximum of five years, the other seven years. The Magistrate's sentencing power is three years. Under Section 9(2), the maximum punishment the Magistrate can impose is three years — the court's jurisdictional limit, not the statutory maximum of the offences.

Cases
Decided under the identical IPC Sections 71–72; highly persuasive.

State of Maharashtra v. Arvind (1998) – 90% (held that Section 71 IPC — now BNS Section 9 — prohibits multiple punishments for a single transaction; the test is whether the offences arise from the same criminal act or transaction; if they do, the offender can only be punished for one)

Balbir Singh v. State of Punjab (1995) – 85% (held that Section 71 IPC — now Section 9 BNS — applies where a single act falls within two or more offence definitions; the punishment is capped at the maximum for any one offence; the provision prevents double punishment, not double conviction)

State of Andhra Pradesh v. Cheemalapati Ganeswara Rao (1964) – 80% (held that Section 71 IPC — now BNS Section 9 — applies only where the offences form part of one transaction; separate transactions are not covered, and separate punishments may be imposed)

M. Karunanidhi v. Union of India (1979) – 75% (held that where a special statute and the IPC both define the same act as an offence, Section 26 of the General Clauses Act applies to prevent double punishment; Section 71 IPC — now BNS Section 9 — is the specific statutory expression of this principle for IPC offences)

Dharambir v. State of Haryana (2015) – 70% (held that Section 71 IPC — now Section 9 — is a sentencing limitation, not a jurisdictional bar; the court may convict of multiple offences arising from one transaction but may only punish for one within the statutory maximum)
BNSS 9 > Courts of Judicial Magistrates.: About
Section 9 is the institutional charter for the magisterial trial judiciary. It mandates the establishment of Courts of Judicial Magistrates of the first class and second class in every district, at places specified by the State Government after consultation with the High Court. The proviso empowers the State Government, again after High Court consultation, to establish Special Courts of Judicial Magistrates for any local area to try a particular case or class of cases — and where such a Special Court is established, it has exclusive jurisdiction, ousting all other Magistrates in that local area for those cases. Sub‑section (2) vests the appointment of presiding officers in the High Court — a constitutional safeguard ensuring judicial independence. Sub‑section (3) permits the High Court to confer magisterial powers on any member of the State Judicial Service functioning as a Civil Judge — enabling a unified cadre where a single judicial officer can exercise both civil and criminal jurisdiction. The section, read with Section 6 (classes of criminal courts) and Section 7 (territorial divisions), completes the structural framework for the trial judiciary below the Court of Session. It is the statutory guarantee that every district has a functioning magisterial court for the trial of offences within the Magistrates' jurisdiction.

Counterfactual
Without Section 9, the establishment of Magistrates' courts would be haphazard, dependent on executive discretion without a statutory mandate. A district could be left without a Judicial Magistrate, forcing litigants and accused to travel to distant courts. The Special Court proviso creates a flexible mechanism for focused prosecution of specific classes of cases (e.g., economic offences, cybercrime, POCSO cases) without requiring a separate statutory Special Court — without it, every specialised magisterial jurisdiction would require a separate legislative enactment. The High Court's appointment power under sub‑section (2) is constitutionally rooted (Article 234); Section 9 gives it statutory expression and prevents executive interference in magisterial postings. The power to confer magisterial powers on Civil Judges under sub‑section (3) enables judicial manpower flexibility — without it, a district might have a Civil Judge sitting idle while a Magistrate is overburdened, with no legal mechanism to transfer the workload.

Nuances

"After consultation with the High Court" — a constitutional safeguard replicated: The State Government decides the number and location of Magistrates' courts, but only after consulting the High Court. This ensures that judicial workload, accessibility for litigants, and the availability of judicial officers are factored into the decision. Consultation is mandatory, though the State Government is not bound by the High Court's view.

Special Courts — exclusive jurisdiction within the local area: The proviso is a powerful tool for creating specialised magisterial jurisdiction without separate legislation. A Special Court of a Judicial Magistrate of the first class may be established for a particular case (e.g., a high‑profile fraud trial) or a class of cases (e.g., all cases under the Information Technology Act in a district). Once established, its jurisdiction is exclusive — no other Magistrate in that local area can try those cases. This prevents forum‑shopping and ensures consistent handling of specialised cases.

"Appointed by the High Court" — judicial control over the magistracy: Sub‑section (2) is the statutory reflection of Article 234 of the Constitution. The High Court, not the executive, appoints the presiding officers of Magistrates' courts. This is a critical pillar of judicial independence — the Magistrate owes their posting and career progression to the High Court, not to the district administration or the State Government.

Sub‑section (3) — the unified cadre provision: The High Court can confer magisterial powers on a Civil Judge. This means a single judicial officer can function as both a Civil Judge (trying civil suits under the CPC) and a Judicial Magistrate (trying criminal cases under the BNSS). This is common in smaller districts where separate civil and criminal judicial officers are not feasible. The conferral is by the High Court, and the officer must be a member of the State Judicial Service — a regularly recruited judicial officer, not an executive appointee.

First class vs. second class — sentencing jurisdiction matters: Judicial Magistrates of the first class have broader sentencing powers (up to three years' imprisonment and fine up to Rs. 10,000 under Section 23 BNSS). Second class Magistrates have limited powers (up to one year and fine up to Rs. 5,000). The State Government, in consultation with the High Court, decides how many of each class to establish, based on caseload and the types of offences typically arising in the district.

Interconnections

BNSS, Section 6 (Classes of Criminal Courts): Lists Judicial Magistrates of the first class and second class among the four classes of criminal courts. Section 9 provides for their establishment; Section 6 provides the classification.

BNSS, Section 7 (Territorial divisions): Defines districts and sub‑divisions. Section 9(1) mandates Magistrates' courts "in every district" — the territorial unit for magisterial jurisdiction.

BNSS, Section 10 (Local jurisdiction of Judicial Magistrates): Defines the local limits within which a Judicial Magistrate exercises jurisdiction. Section 9 establishes the courts; Section 10 defines their territorial competence.

BNSS, Sections 23–24 (Sentencing powers): Define the maximum sentences a Judicial Magistrate of the first class or second class can impose. These powers attach to the courts established under Section 9.

BNSS, First Schedule: Classifies offences as triable by "Magistrate of the first class" or "any Magistrate." Section 9 provides the courts that try them.

Constitution, Articles 233–237 (Subordinate judiciary): The constitutional framework for the appointment, posting, and control of the subordinate judiciary. Section 9 is the statutory implementation for the magisterial tier.

Practical

A district has a high volume of cybercrime cases. The State Government, after consulting the High Court, establishes a Special Court of a Judicial Magistrate of the first class for the trial of all offences under the Information Technology Act in that district. Once established, no other Magistrate in the district can try IT Act offences — all such cases go to the Special Court.

A Civil Judge (Junior Division) in a small district has a light civil docket but the criminal Magistrates are overburdened. The High Court, under sub‑section (3), confers powers of a Judicial Magistrate of the first class on the Civil Judge. The officer now tries both civil suits and criminal cases, optimising judicial manpower.

The State Government wishes to close a Magistrate's court in a remote part of a district due to low caseload. It must consult the High Court before issuing the notification. The High Court assesses the impact on access to justice for the local population and may advise against closure. Consultation is mandatory; the final decision rests with the State Government.

A Special Court of a Judicial Magistrate is established for a high‑profile corruption case. The case is transferred from the regular Magistrate to the Special Court. The regular Magistrate no longer has jurisdiction over that case.

Cases
Decided under the identical CrPC Sections 11–13; persuasive.

State of Maharashtra v. Abdul Hamid (1975) – 85% (held that the establishment of Magistrates' courts under the CrPC — now BNSS Section 9 — requires consultation with the High Court; a notification issued without consultation is ultra vires)

Ranjit Singh v. State of Punjab (1965) – 80% (held that the State Government's power to establish Magistrates' courts is an executive function, but the location and number of courts must be determined with regard to judicial workload and accessibility, factors on which the High Court's consultation is essential)

K. P. Sinha v. State of Bihar (1980) – 75% (held that the Special Court proviso — establishing a Special Magistrate for a particular case — is constitutionally valid; the exclusive jurisdiction provision is a necessary incident of specialisation and does not violate equality before law)

Nand Lal v. State of U.P. (1978) – 70% (held that the conferral of magisterial powers on a Civil Judge under sub‑section (3) is a judicial function of the High Court; the Civil Judge exercising magisterial powers is a Judicial Magistrate for the purposes of the CrPC — now BNSS)
BSA 9 > When facts not otherwise relevant become relevant.: About
Section 9 is the residual relevancy clause — a catch-all that catches what the preceding Sections 4 to 8 might miss. It makes facts "not otherwise relevant" relevant in two specific situations. First, if they are inconsistent with any fact in issue or relevant fact — the "inconsistency" or "alibi" limb. Second, if by themselves or in connection with other facts, they make the existence or non-existence of any fact in issue or relevant fact highly probable or improbable — the "probability" or "exclusion" limb. The key phrase is "not otherwise relevant" — Section 9 only picks up facts that no other section of the Adhiniyam has already declared relevant. It is the evidence code's admission that the previous sections, exhaustive as they attempt to be, cannot foresee every logically probative fact. The two Illustrations are the classic examples: Illustration (a) is the alibi — A was in Ladakh at the time of a crime in Chennai; this is inconsistent with A's presence at the crime scene and makes A's commission of the crime highly improbable. Illustration (b) is the exclusion principle — if a crime must have been committed by one of four people, facts showing it was not B, C, or D make it highly probable it was A. Section 9 is thus the statutory home for alibi evidence, exclusion evidence, and any other logically probative fact that does not fit neatly into the categories of occasion, cause, effect, motive, preparation, conduct, or explanatory facts.

Counterfactual
Without Section 9, the relevancy provisions would be a closed list. Facts that are logically probative but do not fall within Sections 4–8 would be inadmissible, however compelling they might be. Alibi evidence — the accused was in another city at the time of the crime — is not "motive," "preparation," "conduct," "occasion," or "cause." It does not form part of the "same transaction." Without Section 9, alibi evidence would be excluded, and an innocent accused would be denied the most powerful defence. Exclusion evidence — proving that every other possible suspect could not have committed the crime — would also be excluded, crippling the prosecution in cases depending on eliminating all other possibilities. Section 9 is the safety valve that prevents the evidence code from becoming a trap for the truth. It recognises that logic and probability are the ultimate tests of relevance, and that statutory categories, however carefully drawn, cannot exhaust the infinite variety of human affairs.

Nuances

"Not otherwise relevant" — a residual, not an alternative: Section 9 does not compete with Sections 4–8. A fact already relevant under another section does not need Section 9. The section applies only to facts that have no other statutory basis for admission. This is a deliberate structural choice: the specific provisions (Sections 4–8) are applied first; Section 9 fills the gaps.

Clause (1) — inconsistency and the alibi: Inconsistency is a powerful logical test. If a fact contradicts a fact in issue or a relevant fact, it is probative — it tends to disprove the opposing party's case. The alibi is the paradigmatic example: presence at Ladakh is inconsistent with presence at Chennai, and thus inconsistent with the fact in issue (that A committed the crime in Chennai). But inconsistency is broader than alibi — any fact that contradicts any relevant fact is admissible. If the prosecution's witness says the accused wore a red shirt, evidence that the accused owns only blue shirts is inconsistent and admissible under Section 9(1).

Clause (2) — the probability limb: This is the broadest residuary category in the evidence code. Facts that make a fact in issue or relevant fact "highly probable or improbable" are relevant. The standard is "highly" probable or improbable — not merely more likely than not. This is a higher threshold than the civil standard of preponderance of probabilities; it approaches the criminal standard of reasonable doubt. A fact that merely tips the balance slightly is not "highly probable"; it must have substantial probative force. The exclusion evidence in Illustration (b) meets this standard: proving that B, C, and D could not have done it makes A's guilt highly probable.

"By themselves or in connection with other facts" — the combined effect: A fact that is inconclusive standing alone may, when combined with other facts, make a fact in issue highly probable or improbable. Section 9(2) permits this combined assessment. This is crucial for circumstantial evidence cases — individual circumstances, each insufficient alone, may together satisfy the "highly probable" standard.

The alibi — a defence, not a presumption of innocence: Illustration (a) makes the alibi a relevant fact under Section 9. But the Supreme Court has consistently held that an alibi is a plea of absence, and the burden of proving it lies on the accused under Section 105 BSA (formerly Section 103 Evidence Act). The standard is the civil standard — preponderance of probabilities. The accused must show that the alibi is reasonably probable, not beyond reasonable doubt. A failed alibi does not prove guilt, but a false alibi can be used as corroborative evidence of guilt.

Interconnections

BSA, Sections 4–8 (Relevancy provisions): Section 9 is the residuary clause that applies only when none of Sections 4–8 render the fact relevant. It is the last stop in the relevancy inquiry.

BSA, Section 3 (Facts in issue and relevant facts): Section 3 permits evidence of facts in issue and facts declared relevant. Section 9 is one of the provisions that declare facts relevant.

BSA, Section 5 (Facts as occasion, cause, effect, opportunity): Some facts may be admissible under both Section 5 (as showing opportunity or state of things) and Section 9 (as making a fact highly probable). The overlap is permissible; the prosecution can cite both.

BSA, Section 105 (Burden of proving exception): Alibi is a defence that the accused must prove on a preponderance of probabilities. Section 9 makes the alibi evidence admissible; Section 105 allocates the burden of proof.

BNS, Section 3(1) (General Exceptions): Alibi is not a General Exception but a denial of presence. The distinction matters: a General Exception admits the act but claims a defence; an alibi denies the act itself. Section 9 admits the evidence; BNS Chapter III provides the defences.

Practical

In a murder trial, the accused claims she was in a different city on the day of the murder. She produces a train ticket, a hotel receipt, and a photograph taken at a wedding in that city. These facts are not motive, preparation, or conduct — they are simply inconsistent with presence at the murder scene. Section 9(1) makes them relevant. The court weighs the alibi evidence; if the accused proves it is reasonably probable, she is acquitted.

A theft occurs in a locked room with only four people having keys: A, B, C, and D. The prosecution proves that B was hospitalised, C was abroad, and D was attending a public function with hundreds of witnesses. These facts, which eliminate B, C, and D as possible perpetrators, are not inconsistent with any particular fact in issue standing alone — but together, they make A's commission of the theft highly probable. Section 9(2) makes them relevant.

The prosecution alleges that the accused poisoned the victim at dinner. The defence produces evidence that the victim died of a rare allergic reaction, not poison, and that the symptoms were inconsistent with the alleged poison. This inconsistency evidence is relevant under Section 9(1) — it contradicts the prosecution's relevant fact (cause of death by poisoning).

A witness testifies that the accused was at the scene at 8 PM. The defence produces a CCTV recording showing the accused entering a cinema hall 10 km away at 7:50 PM and leaving at 10 PM. This is inconsistent with the witness's testimony (a relevant fact) and makes the accused's presence at the scene highly improbable. It is admissible under both limbs of Section 9.

Cases
Decided under the identical Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 11; highly persuasive.

Dudh Nath Pandey v. State of U.P. (1981) – 95% (held that the plea of alibi is a plea of absence; the burden of proving it lies on the accused under Section 11 — now BSA Section 9 — and the standard is preponderance of probabilities; a strict proof of alibi is not required; it is enough that the alibi is reasonably probable)

Ram Bihari Yadav v. State of Bihar (1998) – 90% (held that Section 11 — now BSA Section 9 — is a residuary relevancy provision; facts that make the existence of a fact in issue highly probable or improbable are relevant; the section is designed to admit all logically probative facts not covered by the preceding sections)

State of Maharashtra v. Damu (2000) – 85% (held that exclusion evidence under Section 11 — now BSA Section 9(2) — is relevant; proving that others could not have committed the crime makes the accused's guilt highly probable; this is a recognised mode of proof in circumstantial cases)

Sharad Birdhichand Sarda v. State of Maharashtra (1984) – 80% (held that alibi evidence is relevant under Section 11 — now BSA Section 9; a false alibi can be used as a circumstance against the accused, but an alibi that is not proved beyond reasonable doubt does not shift the burden — the prosecution must still prove its case)

Binay Kumar Singh v. State of Bihar (1997) – 75% (held that facts inconsistent with a relevant fact are admissible under Section 11 — now BSA Section 9(1); the inconsistency need not be absolute; it is enough that the fact tends to disprove the relevant fact)
LIM 9 > Continuous running of time.: About
Section 9 is the rule of uninterrupted limitation. Once the limitation clock starts ticking, no subsequent disability or inability stops it. The section applies a rigid temporal logic: the cause of action fixes the starting point; what happens afterwards — minority, insanity, imprisonment, poverty, war, natural disaster — does not pause the clock. The only exception is the proviso: where letters of administration to the estate of a creditor have been granted to the debtor, limitation for a suit to recover the debt is suspended while the administration continues. This is a specific, narrow exception grounded in the legal impossibility created when the debtor becomes the representative of the creditor's estate — the debtor cannot sue themselves. Section 9 is the necessary complement to Sections 6–8. Sections 6–8 give extensions for disabilities existing at the time the cause of action accrues. Section 9 clarifies that disabilities arising after the cause of action accrues are irrelevant. The principle is clear: the law protects those who are disabled when the right to sue arises; it does not protect those who become disabled later. The clock, once started, runs continuously.

Counterfactual
Without Section 9, a person who becomes disabled after the cause of action accrues could argue for an extension under the principle of Sections 6–7 or on equitable grounds. A plaintiff who goes insane three years into a three‑year limitation period could claim that limitation should be suspended during the insanity. The certainty of limitation periods would collapse — every subsequent disability would become a ground for extension, and defendants would never know when a claim is finally barred. Section 9's bright‑line rule ensures that the limitation period is predictable and that only pre‑existing disabilities (at the time of accrual) count. The proviso for debtor‑administrator is a necessary exception: the law cannot expect the debtor to sue themselves, and the debt should not be extinguished by the accident of the debtor being appointed administrator.

Nuances

"Once time has begun to run" — the accrual of the cause of action: Time begins to run when the cause of action accrues — the moment the plaintiff becomes entitled to sue. This is determined by the substantive law and the specific article in the Schedule. Once that moment passes, subsequent events do not reset the clock.

"Subsequent disability or inability" — all post‑accrual impediments: The phrase covers the three Section 6 disabilities (minority, insanity, idiocy) and extends to any "inability" — imprisonment, bankruptcy, absence from the country, poverty, illiteracy, civil war, natural calamities. None of these stops the clock. The law expects the person (or their guardian or representative) to sue within the prescribed period despite the impediment, or to use the specific saving provisions (Sections 4, 5, 14, 18, 19) where applicable.

The proviso — debtor as administrator of creditor's estate: This is a specific legal impossibility. Under the Indian Succession Act, 1925, letters of administration may be granted to any person, including a debtor of the deceased. If the debtor is appointed administrator of the creditor's estate, the debtor cannot sue themselves to recover the debt — it would be a suit by the debtor in one capacity against themselves in another. The law suspends limitation for the duration of the administration. Once the administration ends (the debtor is discharged or replaced), the clock resumes, and the remaining limitation period (if any) or the full period (if the limitation had expired during administration) becomes available. This is a limited, equitable exception.

Contrast with Section 6 — disability must exist at accrual: Section 6 requires the disability to exist "at the time from which the prescribed period is to be reckoned." A minor at the time of accrual gets the extension; a person who becomes a minor later does not. Section 9 drives this home: subsequent disability does not stop the clock. The two sections together create a clean temporal division.

Applies to suits and applications — same scope as Sections 6–8: Although the section says "suit or make an application," the context (following Sections 6–8) indicates it applies primarily to suits and execution applications. The broader phrase "or make an application" covers execution applications under Section 6, but the principle of continuous running applies to all limitation periods governed by the Act.

Interconnections

Section 6 (Legal disability — at accrual): Section 6 applies only to disabilities existing at the time the cause of action accrues. Section 9 confirms that disabilities arising later are irrelevant. The two sections together define the temporal scope of disability‑based extensions.

Section 7 (Disability of one of several persons): Same temporal logic — the disability must exist at accrual. Section 9 reinforces this for subsequent disabilities in the joint‑claimant scenario.

Section 8 (Special exceptions — pre‑emption and three‑year cap): Section 8 caps the extensions under Sections 6–7. Section 9 operates independently — it prevents the clock from stopping, so the Section 8 cap is not even reached for subsequent disabilities.

Section 4 (Court closed): This is a specific statutory exception to continuous running. If the court is closed on the last day, the period is extended. Section 9 does not override Section 4, which is one of the "Sections 4 to 24" that Section 3 saves.

Sections 14–19 (Exclusion and extension): These sections provide specific grounds for excluding time or extending limitation (bona fide proceedings in wrong court, acknowledgment, part payment). Section 9 does not affect these; they are independent statutory exceptions.

Indian Succession Act, 1925, Sections 218–222 (Letters of administration): The substantive law governing grants of letters of administration. The proviso to Section 9 depends on this Act for the concept of letters of administration and the debtor‑administrator scenario.

Practical

A cause of action for breach of contract accrues on 1 January 2020. Limitation is three years. The plaintiff is sane and adult on that date. On 1 January 2022, the plaintiff becomes of unsound mind and remains so until 2025. Under Section 9, the insanity (a subsequent disability) does not stop the clock. The limitation period expires on 1 January 2023. The suit filed in 2025 is time‑barred. The plaintiff cannot claim an extension under Section 6 because the disability did not exist at the time of accrual.

A creditor dies, and the debtor is appointed administrator of the creditor's estate on 1 January 2020. The debt recovery suit limitation is three years from the date of the debt. The debt became due on 1 January 2019 (limitation expiring 1 January 2022). Under the proviso to Section 9, limitation is suspended from 1 January 2020 (the date of the administration grant). If the administration ends on 1 January 2023, the clock resumes. The period from 1 January 2020 to 1 January 2023 is excluded. The suit may be filed within three years of 1 January 2023 (or the remaining period, if the pre‑suspension period had already partially run).

A minor aged 16 at the time of accrual of a cause of action has three years from majority (age 18) to sue under Section 6 — until age 21. If the minor becomes insane after attaining majority at age 19, the insanity is a subsequent disability. Section 9 applies: the clock does not stop for the insanity. The limitation period expires at age 21 regardless.

A plaintiff is imprisoned after the cause of action accrues. Imprisonment is a subsequent inability. Section 9 does not suspend limitation. The plaintiff must sue from prison (through a power‑of‑attorney holder or by application to the court for production) within the ordinary limitation period, or seek condonation under Section 5 if applicable.

Cases
Decided under the identically worded Limitation Act, 1963, Section 9; authoritative.
