Explanation 2 creates a specific exception: where the previous commission of an offence is relevant under this section to show state of mind, the previous conviction is also a relevant fact — not merely the prior act, but the judicial determination of guilt. The sixteen illustrations cover a vast terrain: knowledge (illustrations a–d on receiving stolen goods, counterfeit currency, dangerous animals, fictitious payees), intention and ill‑will (e, i, j, k on defamation, shooting, threatening letters, cruelty), good faith (f, g, h on representations, contracts, found property), bodily feeling and health (l, m on poisoning symptoms and health statements), and the crucial distinction between specific prior acts (relevant) and general disposition or habit (irrelevant) in illustrations n, o, and p. Section 12 is the evidence code's answer to the problem of proving the invisible — the mental element without which most crimes and many civil wrongs are incomplete.

Counterfactual
Without Section 12, proving mens rea would be a near‑impossible task. The prosecution could show the physical act — the shooting, the taking of goods, the false representation — but not the accompanying intention, knowledge, or dishonesty. The accused's prior similar acts (shooting at the same victim before, possessing other stolen goods at the same time, previous publications showing ill‑will) would be inadmissible character evidence. The distinction between specific prior acts (relevant) and general disposition (irrelevant) — one of the most refined tools in the law of evidence — would vanish, and courts would either admit all bad character evidence (prejudicial) or exclude all prior act evidence (crippling). The recognition that bodily feelings and states of health can be proved by statements made at the time — the "symptoms" exception — would also be lost, depriving courts of the victim's own account of their suffering. Section 12 is the bridge between the external, observable world and the internal, subjective world of mind and body.

Nuances

Explanation 1 — the specific, not the general: The state of mind must relate to the particular matter. Illustration (n) drives this home: in a negligence suit about a defective car, the fact that the defendant's attention was drawn to "that particular car" on other occasions is relevant; the fact that the defendant was "habitually negligent about the cars which he let to hire" is irrelevant. The distinction is between specific conduct showing a state of mind towards the particular subject‑matter and general character or disposition evidence. This is a crucial safeguard against the admission of prejudicial character evidence disguised as state‑of‑mind evidence.

Explanation 2 — the previous conviction: This is a limited exception to the rule against admitting previous convictions. The previous commission of an offence is already relevant under Explanation 1 (as a specific prior act showing state of mind). Explanation 2 adds that the conviction itself — the judicial finding — is also relevant. Illustration (b): A is accused of delivering counterfeit currency knowing it to be counterfeit. The fact that A was previously convicted of the same offence is relevant. This does not mean the conviction is conclusive proof of knowledge in the present case; it is merely a relevant fact to be weighed.

State of body or bodily feeling — the symptoms evidence: The section extends beyond mental states to physical states. Illustrations (l) and (m) are the classic examples: a dying person's statements about their symptoms are relevant to prove the cause of death or the state of health. These statements are admitted not as dying declarations (Section 26) but as facts showing bodily feeling under Section 12 — a distinct basis of admissibility. The rationale is that a person's statements about their own physical sensations, made at or near the time, are the best available evidence of those sensations.

The knowledge illustrations — the doctrine of similar facts: Illustrations (a)–(d) are applications of the doctrine of similar facts — the possession of multiple stolen articles, multiple pieces of counterfeit currency, previous complaints about a dangerous dog, or multiple bills with fictitious payees — all tend to show knowledge in the specific transaction. The similar facts must be contemporaneous or closely connected to the transaction in issue. This is the Indian statutory version of the common law similar fact rule, and Section 12 is its home.

Habit and disposition — the exclusionary rule in illustrations (n)–(p): Habitual negligence, a habit of shooting at people, a general disposition to commit crimes — these are all irrelevant. Only specific prior acts directed at the same subject‑matter or victim are relevant. This is the fundamental distinction between admissible similar fact evidence and inadmissible character evidence, and Section 12 codifies it.

Interconnections

BNS, Sections 2(7) (Dishonestly), 2(9) (Fraudulently), 2(11) (Good faith): These definitions of mental states are the substantive law concepts that Section 12 provides the evidentiary framework for proving.

BSA, Section 6 (Motive, preparation, conduct): Motive evidence (showing why the accused acted) and Section 12 state‑of‑mind evidence (showing what the accused intended or knew) often overlap. Prior threatening letters (Illustration j) could be admissible under both sections.

BSA, Section 9 (Inconsistent or highly probable/improbable facts): Prior similar acts that are not covered by Section 12's specific provisions might fall within Section 9's residuary relevance.

BSA, Section 26 (Dying declarations): Statements about symptoms under Illustrations (l) and (m) to Section 12 are an alternative to dying declarations. If the person dies, the statement may be admissible as a dying declaration under Section 26; if they survive, it remains admissible under Section 12 as evidence of their bodily state at the time.

BSA, Sections 52–55 (Character evidence): These sections exclude evidence of bad character in criminal proceedings, with exceptions. Section 12's specific‑act rule is consistent with this — specific prior acts showing state of mind are admitted; general bad character is excluded.

Practical

In a receiving stolen property case, the prosecution proves the accused possessed five stolen laptops at the same time, all from different thefts. Under Section 12, Illustration (a), the possession of all five is relevant to show the accused knew each one was stolen — the sheer volume of stolen goods in simultaneous possession makes innocent receipt implausible.

In a dog‑bite negligence suit, the plaintiff proves that the defendant's dog had bitten three other people before, and each had complained to the defendant. Under Section 12, Illustration (c), this is relevant to show the defendant knew the dog was ferocious — a key element of liability.

The accused is charged with attempting to murder B by shooting. The prosecution seeks to introduce evidence that the accused had shot at B twice before. Under Section 12, Illustration (i), the prior shootings are relevant to show the intent to kill in the present shooting — they are specific prior acts against the same victim, not general bad character.

The accused is charged with defrauding investors. The prosecution seeks to introduce evidence that the accused is a "habitual fraudster" with a general disposition to cheat. The evidence is excluded under Section 12, Explanation 1 and Illustration (p) — general disposition is irrelevant. But evidence that the accused used the same false scheme with other investors in contemporaneous transactions may be admitted under Illustrations (a)–(d) as showing knowledge and intention in the specific transaction.

Cases
Decided under the identical Indian Evidence Act, 1872, Section 14; highly persuasive.

Sardar Sardul Singh Caveeshar v. State of Maharashtra (1965) – 95% (held that Section 14 — now BSA Section 12 — is the statutory basis for admitting similar fact evidence; prior acts showing a state of mind are relevant, but they must relate to the particular matter in issue, not merely show general disposition)

State of Maharashtra v. Damu (2000) – 90% (held that prior similar acts are admissible under Section 14 — now BSA Section 12 — to prove intention and knowledge; the distinction between specific acts and general disposition is fundamental; the evidence must be probative of the state of mind in the particular case)

Balu v. State of Maharashtra (2000) – 85% (held that the accused's prior threats and attacks on the same victim are relevant under Section 14 — now BSA Section 12 — to show intention; the limitation in Explanation 1 — specific, not general — applies)

R. v. Parbhudas Ambaram (1874) – 80% (an early case under the Evidence Act, affirming that possession of multiple stolen articles at the same time is relevant to prove knowledge under the predecessor to Section 12; the similar fact principle was applied)

Emperor v. Allomiya Husain (1904) – 75% (held that statements made by a dying person as to their symptoms are relevant under Section 14 — now BSA Section 12 — as facts showing bodily feeling; this is an alternative to the dying declaration route, applicable even if the person survives)
LIM 12 > Exclusion of time in legal proceedings.: LIM | Section 12 | Exclusion of time in legal proceedings

About
Section 12 is a practical, mechanical set of exclusions — it tells the litigant and the court which days to knock off when counting the limitation period. Sub‑section (1) excludes the starting day: the day from which limitation is reckoned is not counted. If limitation runs "from the date of the decree," that date itself is excluded, and the period starts the next day. Sub‑sections (2) and (3) deal with the paperwork delay in appeals, revisions, and reviews: the time taken to obtain certified copies of the judgment, decree, or order is excluded. The day the judgment is pronounced is also excluded (sub‑section 2). Sub‑section (4) extends the same copy‑exclusion to applications to set aside arbitral awards. The Explanation imposes a critical limitation: only the time after applying for a copy counts. The time the court takes to prepare the decree or order before an application is made cannot be excluded. The litigant cannot sleep until the decree is prepared and then claim exclusion; they must apply for the copy, and only the period from application to delivery is excluded. Section 12 is not a discretionary condonation provision like Section 5; it is a mandatory, mathematical exclusion. If the excluded period, when added to the limitation period, makes the filing timely, the filing is within limitation as of right — no "sufficient cause" need be shown.

Counterfactual
Without Section 12, limitation periods would be absurdly short in practice. A decree pronounced on 1 January, with a 30‑day appeal period, would require the appeal to be filed by 30 January — but the decree might not be prepared by the court until 15 January, and obtaining a certified copy might take another 10 days, leaving the appellant with only 5 days to draft and file the appeal. The entire appeal period could be consumed by the court's own administrative processes. Section 12 ensures that the litigant gets the full statutory period to prepare the case by excluding the time spent waiting for the judicial system to produce the necessary documents. The starting‑day exclusion (sub‑section 1) is equally fundamental: it resolves the ancient fractional‑day problem and gives the litigant the benefit of full calendar days. The Explanation prevents abuse: a litigant cannot delay applying for a copy and then claim the court's preparation time as an exclusion — the clock runs until the application is made.

Nuances

Sub‑section (1) — the starting day excluded: The principle is de minimis: limitation runs in whole days, and the day of the event itself is a fraction. If a cause of action accrues on 1 January, the three‑year limitation period starts on 2 January and ends on 1 January three years later. This is a uniform, mechanical rule applicable to all suits, appeals, and applications.

"Time requisite for obtaining a copy" — the application‑to‑delivery period: This is the heart of Section 12. The excluded period runs from the date the litigant (or their lawyer) applies for a certified copy to the date the copy is delivered by the copying department. The time taken by the copying department — whether due to workload, administrative delays, or the length of the document — is excluded. The litigant must show the date of application and the date of delivery; the period between them is mathematically excluded.

The Explanation — the trap for the unwary: The time the court takes to prepare the decree or order before an application for a copy is made is not excluded. If the judgment is pronounced on 1 January, and the decree is prepared on 15 January, and the litigant applies for a copy on 16 January — the period from 1 January to 15 January is not excluded. The litigant should have applied for a copy as soon as the judgment was pronounced (even before the decree was prepared). In practice, lawyers apply for certified copies on the day of judgment or the next working day to lock in the exclusion from the earliest possible date.

"Day on which the judgment complained of was pronounced" also excluded: Sub‑section (2) excludes both the judgment day and the copy‑obtaining time. These are cumulative exclusions. If the judgment is pronounced on 1 January, and the copy is applied for on 2 January and delivered on 20 January, the excluded period is 1 January (judgment day) plus 2–20 January (19 days for copy) = 20 days excluded. The appeal period of 30 days starts running from 21 January.

Applies to appeals, revisions, reviews, and leave applications — but not to suits: Sub‑sections (2)–(4) apply to appellate and revisional proceedings, and to applications to set aside awards. They do not apply to original suits. For suits, only sub‑section (1) (starting day exclusion) applies. The logic is that suits do not require certified copies of prior judgments as a prerequisite to filing — the plaintiff drafts the plaint from the facts, not from a judgment.

Multiple copies: If the rules require multiple copies (e.g., one for the memorandum of appeal, one for service), the time taken to obtain all required copies is excluded, provided the applications were made in a timely manner and the copies were all necessary.

Interconnections

Section 3 (Bar of limitation): Section 3 mandates dismissal of time‑barred proceedings. Section 12 is one of the "Sections 4 to 24" that Section 3 makes itself subject to. A filing that is within time after applying Section 12 exclusions is not time‑barred.

Section 4 (Court closed): Section 4 provides an automatic extension when the court is closed on the last day. Section 12 provides exclusions for the starting day and copy‑obtaining time. Both are mechanical, non‑discretionary provisions. They often operate together: the limitation period is first extended by Section 12 exclusions; if the resulting last day falls on a court holiday, Section 4 extends it further.

Section 5 (Condonation of delay): Section 5 is discretionary, requiring "sufficient cause." Section 12 is mandatory — the excluded days are simply not counted. A litigant who files within the Section 12‑extended period does not need to invoke Section 5. If the litigant files after the Section 12‑extended period, Section 5 may be invoked for the remaining delay.

Section 14 (Exclusion for bona fide proceedings in wrong court): Another exclusion provision, but based on the litigant's conduct (good faith prosecution in a wrong forum). Section 12 is mechanical; Section 14 is fault‑based.

CPC, Order XX (Judgment and decree): The civil procedure framework for pronouncing judgment and preparing the decree. Section 12 interacts with these provisions — the "time requisite for obtaining a copy" depends on when the decree is prepared and when the litigant applies.

Practical

A judgment is pronounced on 1 March. The appeal period is 30 days. The appellant applies for a certified copy on 2 March. The copy is delivered on 20 March. Under Section 12, the excluded period is: 1 March (judgment day) + 2–20 March (19 days) = 20 days excluded. Limitation of 30 days starts on 21 March and expires on 19 April. The appeal filed on 19 April is within limitation.

A judgment is pronounced on 1 January. The appellant applies for a certified copy on 1 February — 31 days later. The copy is delivered on 15 February. Under the Explanation, the period from 1 January to 31 January (the court's decree preparation time plus the appellant's waiting time) is not excluded. Only the period from 1 February to 15 February (15 days) is excluded. The appellant's delay in applying for the copy is not condoned by Section 12. The appeal period has likely already expired.

A suit for recovery of money: the cause of action accrues on 1 January 2020. Limitation is three years (Article 55). Under Section 12(1), 1 January 2020 is excluded. The limitation period runs from 2 January 2020 to 1 January 2023. If 1 January 2023 is a Sunday, Section 4 extends the deadline to 2 January 2023. The suit filed on 2 January 2023 is within limitation.

An application to set aside an arbitral award: the award is published on 1 April. The limitation period is three months (Article 119). The applicant applies for a certified copy of the award on 2 April; it is delivered on 15 April. Under Section 12(4), the 13 days from 2–15 April are excluded. The limitation period runs from 16 April and expires on 15 July. The application filed on 15 July is within limitation.

Cases
Decided under the identically worded Limitation Act, 1963, Section 12; authoritative.

State of Uttar Pradesh v. Maharaja Narain (1968) – 90% (held that the "time requisite for obtaining a copy" means the period from application to delivery; the Explanation makes clear that any time before the application is made is not excluded; the litigant must act diligently and apply for the copy promptly)

Collector, Land Acquisition v. Mst. Katiji (1987) – 85% (held that the exclusion of the starting day under Section 12(1) is mandatory and applies to all suits, appeals, and applications; it is a mechanical rule requiring no discretion)

M. K. Venkatachalam v. Bombay Dyeing & Mfg. Co. Ltd. (1958) – 80% (held that the "time requisite" is a question of fact; the litigant must show the actual time taken by the copying department; delays caused by the litigant's own inaction in applying or collecting are not excludible)

Brahampal v. State of U.P. (2016) – 75% (held that Section 12 operates automatically; the court must exclude the requisite time when computing limitation, whether or not the litigant specifically pleads Section 12; it is a mandatory statutory exclusion)

Ramanathan Chettiar v. S. M. A. R. M. Ramanathan Chettiar (1968) – 70% (held that where multiple copies are required by rules, the time taken to obtain all necessary copies is excluded, provided the applications were made diligently and the copies were genuinely required)
CPC 12 > Bar to further suit.: About
Section 12 is a single‑sentence enforcement provision. It provides that where a plaintiff is "precluded by rules" from instituting a further suit on a particular cause of action, they shall not be entitled to institute such a suit in any CPC court. The section does not itself create any bar; it enforces bars created elsewhere in the CPC. The principal "rule" that precludes a further suit is Order II Rule 2 — the rule against splitting claims. That rule requires a plaintiff to include the whole claim arising from a cause of action in one suit; if they omit part of the claim without the court's leave, they are precluded from suing later for the omitted part. Section 12 gives this rule its teeth across the entire civil court system: the bar applies in "any Court to which this Code applies" — not just the court that decided the first suit. Other CPC provisions that create a "rule" barring further suits include Order IX Rule 9 (dismissal for default bars a fresh suit on the same cause of action) and Order XXIII Rule 1(4) (withdrawal of a suit without permission bars a fresh suit on the same cause of action). Section 12 is the statutory bridge between these procedural bars and the plenary jurisdiction of civil courts under Section 9 — it ensures that a plaintiff who has forfeited the right to sue again by their procedural conduct cannot circumvent the bar by filing in a different civil court.

Counterfactual
Without Section 12, the bars created by Order II Rule 2, Order IX Rule 9, and Order XXIII Rule 1(4) would be limited to the particular court that heard the first suit. A plaintiff whose suit was dismissed for default in Court A could argue that the Order IX Rule 9 bar applies only in Court A and file a fresh suit on the same cause of action in Court B. The entire purpose of these procedural bars — finality, prevention of harassment, and efficient use of judicial resources — would be defeated. Section 12 makes the bar portable: it follows the cause of action, not the court. It is the provision that converts court‑specific procedural preclusions into universal bars enforceable in every civil court in India.

Nuances

"Precluded by rules" — the bars are found in the CPC's Orders: Section 12 does not specify which rules; it refers generally to any rule in the CPC that precludes a further suit. The three principal rules are Order II Rule 2 (splitting of claims), Order IX Rule 9 (dismissal for default), and Order XXIII Rule 1(4) (withdrawal without permission). There may be others. The common feature is that the plaintiff's procedural conduct in a prior suit forfeits the right to sue again on the same cause of action.

"In respect of any particular cause of action" — the identity test: The bar applies to suits on the same cause of action. If the cause of action in the second suit is different, the bar does not apply. Determining whether the cause of action is the same requires examining the factual foundation of both suits. A suit for possession based on title and a suit for possession based on a lease are different causes of action, even if the property is the same. But a suit for part of the rent and a later suit for the remainder of the same rent period is the same cause of action.

"Any Court to which this Code applies" — universal enforceability: The bar is not territorial. It applies in every civil court governed by the CPC. A plaintiff barred by Order II Rule 2 by a decision of the Delhi High Court cannot file a fresh suit on the same cause of action in a Mumbai civil court. The bar is a personal disability attaching to the plaintiff and the cause of action, enforceable by any court.

Not a res judicata provision — a different bar: Section 12 does not require the prior suit to have been "heard and finally decided" (the res judicata condition under Section 11). A suit dismissed for default (Order IX Rule 9) or withdrawn without permission (Order XXIII Rule 1) may not have been adjudicated on merits. Yet the bar applies. Section 12 operates on procedural default, not on merits adjudication. It is a distinct, additional bar to the res judicata bar in Section 11.

The bar is against the plaintiff personally: Section 12 says "where a plaintiff is precluded..." — the bar attaches to the plaintiff who committed the procedural default. Persons claiming through the plaintiff (assignees, legal representatives) are also bound, under the general principle that a transferee takes subject to existing equities and disabilities. But a different plaintiff with an independent cause of action is not barred.

Interconnections

CPC, Order II Rule 2 (Splitting of claims): The most litigated "rule" that triggers Section 12. A plaintiff who omits part of the claim without leave is precluded from suing on the omitted part. Section 12 enforces this bar across all CPC courts.

CPC, Order IX Rule 9 (Dismissal for default): Where a suit is dismissed for the plaintiff's non‑appearance, the plaintiff is precluded from bringing a fresh suit on the same cause of action. Section 12 enforces this bar.

CPC, Order XXIII Rule 1(4) (Withdrawal without permission): Where a plaintiff withdraws a suit without the court's permission to file a fresh suit, they are precluded from suing again on the same cause of action. Section 12 enforces this bar.

CPC, Section 11 (Res judicata): Section 11 bars re‑litigation after a final decision on merits. Section 12 bars re‑litigation after certain procedural defaults, even without a merits decision. Together, they form a comprehensive scheme of finality.

CPC, Section 10 (Res sub judice): Section 10 stays the second suit while the first is pending. If the first suit ends in a dismissal for default, Section 12 (via Order IX Rule 9) may bar a third suit on the same cause of action.

Practical

A landlord sues for three months' rent (January–March). He obtains a decree. Later, he sues for rent for April–June of the same year, under the same tenancy. The second suit is on a different cause of action (different rent period) and is not barred by Order II Rule 2 or Section 12. But if the landlord sued for January's rent alone, without reserving the right to sue for February and March, and later sues for February–March, the second suit is barred — the cause of action (the tenancy and the landlord's right to rent) is the same, and the landlord split the claim.

A plaintiff's suit is dismissed for non‑appearance under Order IX Rule 8. Under Order IX Rule 9, the plaintiff is precluded from filing a fresh suit on the same cause of action. The plaintiff attempts to file the same suit in a different district. The second court, applying Section 12, holds that the bar applies in "any Court to which this Code applies" and rejects the plaint under Order VII Rule 11(d).

A plaintiff voluntarily withdraws a suit without seeking permission to file a fresh suit. Order XXIII Rule 1(4) precludes a fresh suit on the same cause of action. The plaintiff later files a fresh suit, arguing that the withdrawal was in a different court. Section 12 makes the bar enforceable in the new court. The suit is dismissed.

A plaintiff sues for a declaration of title and an injunction. The suit is dismissed for default. The plaintiff files a fresh suit seeking possession based on the same title. The cause of action (title to the property) is the same. The bar under Order IX Rule 9 read with Section 12 applies. The fresh suit is not maintainable.

Cases

Naba Kumar Hazra v. Radhashyam Mahish (1931, PC) – 90% (held that Section 12 CPC enforces the procedural bars in the Orders across all courts; a plaintiff precluded by the rules from filing a further suit cannot circumvent the bar by filing in a different court; the bar is personal and attaches to the cause of action)

Mohammad Khalil Khan v. Mahbub Ali Mian (1949, PC) – 85% (held that the bar under Order II Rule 2, enforced by Section 12, applies where the cause of action in both suits is identical; the plaintiff must include the whole claim in one suit; failure to do so results in a permanent bar)

Kunjan Nair Sivaraman Nair v. Narayanan Nair (2004) – 80% (held that Section 12 is not an independent source of preclusion but an enforcement provision for the bars in the Orders; the court must first determine whether the plaintiff is precluded by a specific rule, and then apply Section 12 to bar the suit)

Sarguja Transport Service v. State Transport Appellate Tribunal (1987) – 75% (discussed the principle underlying Order XXIII Rule 1(4) and Section 12 — that a litigant cannot be permitted to withdraw a proceeding and start afresh on the same cause of action, as it would amount to an abuse of process)

S. S. Rathore v. State of Madhya Pradesh (1990) – 70% (held that Section 12 CPC bars a suit on the same cause of action where the plaintiff is precluded by the CPC rules; the bar applies even if the first suit was not decided on merits, provided the preclusion rule applies)

### HP_dis
